==PAGE:3==
Summary
This Master’s thesis aims to apply and automate a proposed compositional veriﬁcation approach on
railway networks which could serve as an alternative to previous veriﬁcation methods. One of the
most popular methods for verifying railway networks utilises model-checking. Although, it is known
to cause state-space explosions given suﬃciently large networks, making them infeasible to verify.
As such this thesis details the development and documentation of a tool that supports compositional
veriﬁcation of railway control systems. This is done using a ground-breaking proof presented in [HF23]
which shows that the safety properties of a network can be veriﬁed by verifying smaller sub-network
networks, if these together make up the original network. By leveraging this proof, this thesis explores
the possibility of automating a decomposition procedure that is able to decompose any railway network
into partial networks that are already known to be safe, thereby verifying the original network. The
procedure is ultimately captured in an algorithm which has been implemented and can be used as
a command-line tool. The command-line tool takes a well formed railway network speciﬁed using
XML as input and returns the found elementary and unbreakable networks as XML ﬁles. The newly
developed command-line tool has been shown to verify the safety properties of networks at a rate
which is orders of magnitude faster than the existing approach and uses a fraction of the memory. As
such the goal for this thesis has been achieved.
i
==PAGE:4==
Resum´e
Denne kandidatafhandling forsøger at anvende og automatisere en foresl˚aet kompositionel veriﬁkation-
smetode p˚a jernbanenetværk, der kan bruges som et alternativ til tidligere veriﬁkationsmetoder. En af
de mest populære metoder til veriﬁcering af jernbanenetværk er anvendelse af model-checking. Dog er
denne metode kendt for at skabe ”state-space explosions” givet tilstrækkeligt store jernbanenetværk,
hvilket gør metoden uholdbar til brug af veriﬁcering af større netværk.
Denne afhandling beskriver udviklingen og dokumentationen af et værktøj, der understøtter kom-
positionel veriﬁkation af jernbanesikringsanlæg.
Dette er gjort ved brug af et banebrydende bevis
præsenteret i [HF23] hvilket viser at sikkerhedsegenskaberne af et netværk kan veriﬁceres ved at veri-
ﬁcere mindre delnetværk, hvis disse tilsammen udgør det originale netværk. Ved anvendelse af dette
bevis, vil denne afhandling udforske muligheden for at automatisere en nedbrydningsprocedure, som
er i stand til at bryde et jernbanenetværk ned til delnetværk, som i forvejen vides at være sikre, og
derved veriﬁcere det originale netværk. Proceduren er endeligt udtrykt ved en algoritme, der er blevet
implementeret og kan blive brugt som et kommandolinjeværktøj. Kommandolinjeværktøjet tager et
velformet jernbanenetværk speciﬁceret i en XML-ﬁl som input og returnerer de fundne elementære og
unedbrydelige netværk som XML-ﬁler. Det nyudviklede kommandolinjeværktøj har vist sig at veriﬁ-
cere sikkerhedsegenskaberne af netværk med en rate, som er ﬂere størrelsesordener hurtigere end den
eksisterende metode og bruger en brøkdel af hukommelsen. Dermed er form˚alet med denne afhandling
opn˚aet.
ii
==PAGE:5==
Preface
This thesis was prepared at DTU Compute, Technical University of Denmark, in fulﬁlment of the re-
quirements for acquiring a M.Sc. degree in Computer Science and Engineering. Under the supervision
of Associate Professor Anne Elisabeth Haxthausen and Professor Alessandro Fantechi at University of
Florence.
The thesis presents the process and results of identifying and specifying an algorithm that can automat-
ically perform compositional veriﬁcation of a given railway network, which is ultimately implemented
as a command-line tool.
In addition to this report, the collection of products created during the course of the project has been
handed in. This includes RSL speciﬁcations of the algorithm, the C++ implementation, as well as test
ﬁles and their outputs. Other materials have been included as well, such as user guides for the tools,
networks speciﬁed in XML-ﬁles and other supplementary ﬁles.
The thesis was written in the period from January 23rd 2023 to June 23rd 2023.
Kongens Lyngby, June 23rd 2023
´Oli K´arason Mikkelsen
Soﬁe-Amalie Petersen

Acknowledgements
We would, ﬁrst of all, like to thank our supervisors Associate Professor Anne Elisabeth Haxthausen
and Professor Alessandro Fantechi for providing and sharing their immense insight, relevant topics,
and providing exceptional guidance throughout the course of the project. They also made available all
the needed tools and ﬁles to complete the tasks at hand. A special thanks to Associate Professor Anne
Elisabeth Haxthausen for making time in her schedule for weekly meetings, discussing, and answering
questions without which, completing the thesis would not have been possible.
Thanks to Gloria Gori for running experiments using existing tools for the veriﬁcation of railway con-
trol systems and for allowing us to use the results. These results gave us points of reference to compare
the results of our tool to which was very valuable.
Thanks to Jan Peleska for providing parts of RT-Tester tool which was the foundation upon which our
ﬁnal tool was built.
Thanks to Anna Nam Anh Nguyen and Ole Benjamin Eilgaard for providing the network decomposer
tool which played an integral role in our tool supporting compositional veriﬁcation of railway control
systems.
Thanks to Cecilie Lindberg and Mikkel Blach Hansen for their XML to RSL parser tool, which helped
signiﬁcantly during testing of our RSL speciﬁcations by eliminating the need for manual translation
of network speciﬁcations.
Thanks to Ejner Fergo for always providing prompt assistance whenever we faced challenges regarding
access to DTU servers or content.
We would each like to thank our friends and families for providing support as well as outside perspec-
tives which helped clarify certain aspects of the report.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Trains have played a vital role in logistics for over 200 years. Ever since, they have only become
more ubiquitous in society. In the year 2023 train accidents unfortunately still occur and often claim
human lives. As such the continued safety of their operations and operational reliability is of utmost
importance. With ever-expanding railway networks, reliable techniques for ensuring safety is a priority.
These safety-critical systems can be veriﬁed using formal methods.
Formal methods have a wide variety of applications and are suitable within the domain of railway
control systems. Especially model checking has been popular as it is fully automated. However, model
checking of railway control systems has considerable limitations in terms of scalability due to the state
space explosion that may occur for railway control systems controlling large railway networks. Many
of these discoveries are a result of the inter-disciplinary research project RobustRailS1, which was a
collaboration between the Technical University of Denmark, Bremen University, Banedanmark, Danish
traﬃc authorities and DSB, which has aimed to develop methods for achieving safe railway operations.
As such, exploring new approaches to verify networks without causing state space explosion are at
the core of this thesis, and will build upon ﬁndings and tools developed as part of RobustRailS. The
articles [FHM17], [MFH17], and [MFH16] by the authors, Fantechi, Haxthausen, and Macedo present
a method for avoiding state space explosion when performing model checking by following a particular
decomposition approach to divide the given network into smaller networks which are cheaper to verify.
This approach has been shown to be sound due to a proof presented in ”Compositional Veriﬁcation
of Railway Interlocking Systems” [HF23] which states that if a network is decomposed following a
particular procedure and the safety properties of all of the resulting sub-networks can be veriﬁed, then
the safety properties of the original network hold. In the article ”Decomposing the Veriﬁcation of
Interlocking Systems” [HFG23a] an idea for applying this decomposition method repeatedly until the
sub-networks have conﬁgurations that have already been veriﬁed. As such, this approach may be able
to serve as an alternative to model checking large networks.
The goal of this project is to explore solutions to the problem of memory explosions when veriﬁcation
is done on increasingly large railway network control systems by realising the decomposition approach
described above. More speciﬁcally, the goal is to create a command-line tool that, given a large railway
network, can systematically decompose the network into smaller networks, the topologies of which,
have previously been veriﬁed by another proven method, e.g. model checking [VHP17]. We conjecture
that this tool will reduce peak memory consumption and execution time, making it possible to verify
increasingly large networks without an exponential rise in needed memory.
The results obtained as part of this thesis have been included in the article ”Automated Compo-
sitional Veriﬁcation of Interlocking Systems” [HFG+23b] which has been accepted for publication for
RSSRail 2023.
1http://www.robustrails.man.dtu.dk/

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
Overview of Chapters
This section presents a summary of the contents in each of the individual chapters. The overall struc-
ture reﬂects the project work chronologically.
Chapter 2 - Methodology This chapter presents methodology adopted during the course of the
project in terms of how each of the chapters and their associated tasks were approached.
Chapter 3 - Background The background chapter gives the necessary introduction to the domain of
railways networks. Furthermore, it elaborates on the compositional veriﬁcation and the decomposition
method. Furthermore, it introduces the decomposition tool and the RAISE Speciﬁcation Language
(RSL).
Chapter 4 - Analysis The analysis presents the problem as well as the approach used to in the pur-
suit of a solution. An introduction to the existing data types and structures of the domain are given.
The process of identifying an algorithm with which to solve the problem is presented. Afterwards, the
precise speciﬁcations of the algorithm are presented and explained as speciﬁed in the RSL.
Chapter 5 - Implementation This chapter presents the C++ implementation based on the speciﬁ-
cations deﬁned in the analysis chapter. The conversion and di↵erences as compared to the RSL version
are the main focal points.
Chapter 6 - RSL Experiments This chapter presents the methodology and approach to the ex-
periments performed on the RSL speciﬁcations and the results are compared to those of the previous
veriﬁcation tool. Other exploratory experiments are also presented and discussed.
Chapter 7 - C++ Experiments This chapter presents the experiments of the implementation of
the tool in C++, as well as the results.
Chapter 8 - Discussion This chapter discusses the di↵erent ﬁndings and observations made based
on the analysis and results and outlines potential avenues for improvements and other aspects that
could be explored further in the future.
Chapter 9 - Conclusion This chapter reﬂects on the goals and conjecture of this thesis and presents
closing remarks.
==PAGE:13==
Chapter 2
Methodology
This chapter presents the overall methodology and approach to the project. As an initial familiari-
sation with the domain is needed, the methodology and approach are divided into two parts, namely
knowledge acquisition and analysis of the problem.
Knowledge Acquisition
The ﬁrst part is naturally the knowledge acquisition of the domain regarding railway networks. Al-
though, the domain of trains and rails is relatable from everyday life, it is important to have a deep
understanding of the terminology used to describe the inner workings of the domain as these are used to
present a distilled version of the railway networks. As such the knowledge acquisition will be comprised
of reading the literature on the domain. The aspects of the domain that are deemed most essential to
the understanding the ﬁndings of this thesis will be presented in the following chapter Background.
Another important, more practical part of the knowledge acquisition has been getting access to the
RobustRailS server at DTU to try the previously implemented tools. Furthermore, it is also through
the RobustRailS server that the source code of these tools are available. Having access to the existing
codebase has been beneﬁcial as an additional source of information.
Approach
The approach for devising an algorithm with which to potentially solve the stated problem, includes
writing precise speciﬁcations.
The RAISE Speciﬁcation Language will be the chosen medium for deﬁning the speciﬁcations, as it is
succinct, readable, unambiguous, and allows for rigorous testing. As such, the transition from iden-
tifying an algorithm to writing the actual speciﬁcations is seamless while maintaining correctness.
Additionally, the speciﬁcations will act as a blueprint for a more performant version of the tool, im-
plemented in a general-purpose language.
A rigorous translation approach from the RSL speciﬁcations to the chosen general-purpose language
has been devised and will be strictly followed.
During development of both versions, a test driven approach is adopted, such that each new function-
ality added is thoroughly tested.
Finally experiments will be conducted using each version, and the results and performance will be
evaluated and analysed.

==PAGE:14==
Chapter 3
Background
This Chapter provides an introduction to the subject of railway networks and their attributes. Fur-
thermore, explaining how railway networks are translated from the real world to a domain speciﬁc
model to ensure safety properties are enforced.
Railway networks in the real world may have curves, tunnels, bridges, level-crossings, switches, and
other elements which can impact the course of a train.
Therefore, when it comes to representing
the railway network for modelling, a more distilled version is used, which makes it possible to model
a network, using only three types of components, namely linear sections, point sections and marker
boards while still capturing all critical safety properties. The following Section gives an introduction
to the distilled representation of railway networks and their components.
3.1
Railway Network
A railway network also referred to as a network is made up of linear sections, point sections and marker
boards, sometimes referred to as signals. As rails are bi-directional, a network can be interpreted
from any direction. However, there are always two possible and opposite directions, as in, a train
can travel forwards or backwards on any given track. To keep these directions consistent, regardless
of environment or geographic location, they will be referred to as UP and DOWN. In this thesis, the
convention followed is such, that the DOWN direction points to the left of the page, and UP direction
points to the right. Figure 3.1 shows a simple network. One can imagine the network representing a
part of a train station where di↵erent tracks all merge into one track before pulling up to or out of the
train station.
Figure 3.1: A small network comprised of two point, eight linears and nine marker boards.
The network shown in Figure 3.1 consists of all the di↵erent components available for modelling
a network. The network is compromised of nine linear sections, two points and nine marker boards.
4
==PAGE:15==
3.1. RAILWAY NETWORK
5
The marker board mb1 signiﬁes whether it is safe for a train to enter in the UP direction, whereas mb2
placement signiﬁes if it is safe to exit in the DOWN direction. The dotted lines are used to indicate a
border section but can also signify that the network may continue beyond the depiction. Typically for
sample networks, linear sections are labelled with the preﬁx t followed by a number, while points are
labelled with the preﬁx p followed by a number.
3.1.1
Linear
A Linear, as the name suggests, represents a linear section in the network. As such, they have two
ends, each of which may be connected to another section. These ends are deﬁned as UP and DOWN
corresponding to the directions described above. Consider the linear section t2 seen in Figure 3.1.
Each end of the section is represented by a vertical line. Furthermore, it has t1 as its DOWN neighbour
and p1 as its UP neighbour.
As mentioned, the DOWN and UP directions, as annotated in Figure 3.1, are shown as left and right
respectively. So, if a linear section has a neighbour in its DOWN direction, it means that the neighbour
is placed to the left of it, graphically, whereas having a neighbour in the UP direction means that the
neighbour is placed to the right. An example of this can be seen in Figure 3.1 where the linear t4
has t3 in its DOWN direction and p1 in its UP direction. When a Linear only has a single neighbour in
either the UP or DOWN direction, it is called a Border.
3.1.2
Point
A Point is a section that represents a railway switch. Therefore, a Point has three ends. The point-ends
are as follows: STEM, PLUS and MINUS. Point-ends are sometimes referred to as the sides of a point.
Figure 3.2: The terminology describing the di↵erent parts of a point.
Figure3.2 shows the terminology used to describe a point. As shown, the STEM is the end that splits
into PLUS and MINUS. The PLUS end is the branch, which is the continuation of the STEM, whereas the
MINUS end is the diverging branch. This is merely a chosen convention, as opposed to the real world
where both branches can be diverging. An example of a point can be seen in 3.1 with label p1 and
has the following neighbours:
The STEM neighbour of p1 is p2
The PLUS neighbour of p1 is t4
The MINUS neighbour of p1 is t2
Point-ends are either connected to a linear section or to another point. A point can never be a border
i.e. a point must have a neighbour at all point-ends. A small but signiﬁcant detail is, that traﬃc from
either PLUS or MINUS will always be connected to STEM. This is due to the fact that in reality, the point
consists of a railroad switch where PLUS and MINUS share the rail that shifts the position between the
two.
==PAGE:16==
6
CHAPTER 3. BACKGROUND
It is possible to imagine points in the real world having more than two branches as shown in Figure
3.3a). However, it is also possible to represent such conﬁgurations with points having only two branches
each. Figure 3.3b) shows how such conﬁgurations can be reduced to networks that only utilise points as
described in this section that conform to the domain standards, namely having exactly three point-ends
and neighbours.
Figure 3.3: Diagram showing how a point with more than three neighbours can be transformed into
multiple points to ﬁt within the domain with exactly three neighbours each.
Speciﬁcally, Figure 3.3 shows how a point with three branches (four total neighbours), can be
reduced to points with only two branches (three total neighbours). The reduction approach can be
generalised such that for each additional branch that a point should have, an intermediate point can
be introduced. That is, a point which should have n branches can be represented by chaining together
n - 1 points like shown in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Generalisation of how a point with multiple branches can be constructed from basic points.
3.1.3
Track Sections
Track sections is a term used when referring to both linear and point sections. As such when track
sections are mentioned in this thesis, they will only refer to either of these.
==PAGE:17==
3.1. RAILWAY NETWORK
7
3.1.4
Marker Board
Marker boards constitute an important safety property in networks, as they indicate to a train driver,
whether it is safe to enter the next stretch, or if it has been reserved by another train. As such marker
boards have two positions, open or closed. This feature is not explicitly considered in this thesis,
however, their mere placement is enough to guarantee that the safety properties of the network can
be met, granted one has control of the marker boards.
Marker boards may lock a stretch for other trains, disallowing them from entering in the opposite
direction in order to prevent head-on collisions. Sections may also be locked for trains travelling in the
same direction, e.g. to prevent a train from merging onto a point further ahead which is currently in
use by another train. As such, marker boards enable multiple routes utilising the same track sections
to co-exist by controlling when access is given to di↵erent trains.
Marker boards can only be placed on linear sections. Each linear section can have up to two marker
boards, one at each end as shown in Figure 3.1 on page 4.
on the linear section t7.
A common
conﬁguration has a marker board be placed on two separate linear sections, as seen in Figure 3.1
on page 4, where the marker board mb1 is pointing to its neighbouring section t2, while said linear
section’s marker board mb2 is pointing back towards t1. This is used to indicate whether it is safe
for the train to enter the section in their respective directions. In Figure 3.1 marker board mb1 is
only visible for the train conductor when going in the UP direction, whereas marker mb2 is only visible
in the DOWN direction, as indicated by the arrows of the marker boards. While marker boards can be
placed on any linear section, they are required at all borders as seen in Figure 3.1 in order to guarantee
the safety properties of a network. This is just one requirement of many to ensure networks’ safety
properties. The other requirements will be presented in the following section.
3.1.5
Well Formed Networks
This section gives an introduction to the formal requirements of networks as outlined by Linh H. Vu in
”Formal Development and Veriﬁcation of Railway Control System” [Vu15]. There are 7 requirements,
each requirement is considered of equal importance within the domain. The importance of working
with well formed networks is due to their attributes being necessary for safety to be guaranteed.
A network can be classiﬁed as well formed if it conforms to all the requirements of the domain.
Networks that do not conform to the requirements of the domain are not considered since their safety
properties cannot necessarily be guaranteed.
The requirements for well formed networks are deﬁned in [Vu15] Section 4.2.1 ”Railway Network Lay-
outs”, and are summarised below.
N-01: The ﬁrst requirement is that all elements in the network must have unique identiﬁers, i.e.
no two linear sections, points, or marker boards may have the same identiﬁer.
N-02, N-03, N-04: Each element type, linear sections, points, and marker boards, have their
own requirements to be well formed, which are deﬁned in [Vu15]. As such every element in
a network must fulﬁl their own wellformedness requirements for the network as a whole to be
considered well formed.
N-05: Another requirement is that the orientation of the network must be consistent. That is,
a train that enters the network, travelling in a given direction (UP or DOWN), must at no point
ﬁnd itself travelling in the other direction while having kept its forward momentum.
N-06: The penultimate requirement for wellformedness is that no cycles can be present in a
network. Notably, this does not relate to topological loops, but rather that when travelling along
==PAGE:18==
8
CHAPTER 3. BACKGROUND
a valid route, a train should not pass any section more than once.
N-07: The last wellformedness requirement presented in [Vu15] is whether a given network has a
so-called well formed boundary conﬁguration. That is, every border section must have exactly one
marker board that points towards its only neighbouring section, and said neighbouring section
must have at least one marker board which points towards the aforementioned border section.
An example of requirement N-07 being fulﬁlled can be seen in Figure 3.5, where the border sections
t1 and t3 each have one marker board pointing towards their only neighbour t2 which, in turn, has a
marker board pointed in each direction towards the neighbouring borders. The reason it is important
for the boundary conﬁguration to be satisﬁed, is that it ensures the possibility to control traﬃc going
in and out of the network, regardless of how a neighbouring network might be conﬁgured.
With the above enumerated requirements, it follows that the smallest well formed network that exists
within the domain must take the form as is depicted in Figure 3.5 which consists of three linears and
four marker boards.
Figure 3.5: Smallest possible well formed network.
The smallest well formed network containing a point is shown in Figure 3.6 consisting of a point, six
linear sections, and six marker boards.
Figure 3.6: Smallest possible well formed network using a single point.
A particular subset of well formed networks, elementary networks, which will play an important
role in the compositional veriﬁcation of networks will be explored in the following section.
==PAGE:19==
3.2. ELEMENTARY NETWORKS
9
3.2
Elementary Networks
This section describes the possible conﬁguration of networks which are considered elementary.
For a network to be considered elementary, a few criteria must be met. Firstly, elementary networks
must conform to the well formed criteria and must either consist of only linear sections, i.e. contain
no points, or it can contain exactly one point. The allowed conﬁgurations for elementary networks can
be seen in Figure 3.7.
Figure 3.7: All possible elementary network conﬁgurations. Inspired by [HFG23a]
In the case of an elementary network containing only linear sections, exactly four marker boards
are required as demanded by the boundary conﬁguration constraint. As illustrated in Figure 3.7 b),
the network can consist of an arbitrary number of linear sections, as long as there are no marker boards
aside from the four previously mentioned.
In the case of the network containing a point, each neighbouring section must ultimately lead to
a valid boundary conﬁguration as shown in Figure 3.7 c) and d). As a result, such a network must
necessarily contain at least six marker boards. Apart from those, the only valid positions, for marker
boards to be placed, are on the direct neighbours of the point, with the signal pointing towards the
point as shown in Figure 3.7 c) As such, elementary networks containing a point may contain between
6-9 marker boards. There may exist an arbitrary number of linear sections between a boundary and
point as long as no marker boards are placed on any of the intermediate linear sections, with the only
exception being those 6-9 marker boards described above.
There can from the above explanation exist many di↵erent conﬁgurations of elementary networks.
==PAGE:20==
10
CHAPTER 3. BACKGROUND
3.3
Compositional Veriﬁcation
This section focuses on explaining compositional veriﬁcation which constitutes an important role in
solving the problem of state space explosion.
In the article ”Compositional Veriﬁcation of Railway Interlocking Systems” [HF23], the authors pro-
vide a proof of the validity of compositional veriﬁcation. That is, they prove that to verify any network
that can be split into two sub-networks, it is suﬃcient to verify the sub-networks separately.
This discovery is signiﬁcant since it means that large networks, which would result in state-space ex-
plosion when veriﬁed in the traditional manner, can now be veriﬁed via their smaller sub-networks.
Assuming that the sub-networks are further divisible, it would be possible to repeatedly break the
resulting networks into smaller and smaller, more easily veriﬁable networks, until the resulting sub-
networks are either indivisible or resemble a network which has already been veriﬁed using the tradi-
tional model checking approach.
The elementary networks described in Section 3.2 Elementary Networks are examples of such
previously veriﬁed networks. It should be possible for any arbitrary well formed network conﬁguration
to be broken down, in such a way that they only yield elementary or indivisible networks.
Figure 3.8: Diagram showing two sub-networks as a result of separating a network.
This assertion might not appear immediately obvious. A seemingly trivial counter-example would be
to separate a network between two points. E.g. if the network shown in Figure 3.1 on page 4 were to be
separated between the points p1 and p2, the resulting sub-networks would not fulﬁl the requirements
of elementary networks. The sub-networks in question can be seen in Figure 3.8. In fact, the sub-
networks would not even be well formed, since the points p1 and p2 would be lacking neighbours at
their STEM and PLUS side respectively. As such, both networks violate the boundary conﬁguration
requirement.
Therefore, a systematic decomposition approach has previously been devised which
ensures the wellformedness of resulting sub-networks, given that the parent network is also well formed.
The decomposition procedure is described in the following section.
3.4
Decomposition
Decomposition refers to the process of dividing a given network into two partial networks while re-
taining the properties necessary to maintain wellformedness for both sub-networks. It is required that
the original network, which is to be decomposed, must be well formed initially. As such, it can be
assumed that the elements that are not immediately adjacent to the point of separation retain their
individual properties and will, therefore, not be the cause of any wellformedness violations. Rather,
if any violations are present they must necessarily be localised around the place at which the original
network was separated as can be seen in the example shown in Figure 3.8 where the points p1 and p2
are the cause of the wellformedness violations, while all other sections and marker boards still conform
to the requirements.
==PAGE:21==
3.4. DECOMPOSITION
11
In order to describe decomposition accurately, the way in which networks are separated must ﬁrst be
formalised. The separation is referred to as a cut, and the place at which a cut should be performed
is speciﬁed using a cut speciﬁcation.
3.4.1
Cut Speciﬁcations
A cut is a speciﬁcation that represents locations where a network can be divided into two sub-networks.
In the domain, track sections are treated as being atomic, that is, they are indivisible. Therefore, cuts
can only exist between two adjacent track sections and can therefore be represented by the sections on
both sides of the cut. In the example on Figure 3.8, the cut speciﬁcation can be represented as: (p1, p2),
while a cut speciﬁcation like (t2, t4) would not be valid since the sections t2 and t4 are not adjacent.
As with networks, there exists a set of requirements that must be fulﬁlled for a cut speciﬁcation
to be well formed.
These are presented by Anna Nam Anh Nguyen and Ole Benjamin Eilgaard
in ”Development and Use of a Tool Supporting Compositional Veriﬁcation of Railway Interlocking
Systems” [NE20a] Section 3.5. These are quoted verbatim as follows:
• GCC-1. A general cut is made between two sections.
• GCC-2. The two sections are a part of the given network.
• GCC-3. The two sections are distinct.
• GCC-4. The two sections are neighbours. Note that this will imply GCC-2 and GCC-3.
• GCC-5. None of the two sections are a border section
There are several types of cuts to be considered, as it is not always possible to cleanly separate a
network into two distinct sub-networks like in the example above.
Figure 3.9: The di↵erent types of cuts that can be performed on a network.
Figure 3.9 shows the three di↵erent cut types that exist within the domain. Each di↵erently coloured
line signiﬁes a di↵erent type of cut. Common for all of the cuts is the fact that they are wrapped in a
pair of curly brackets, this is to indicate that each cut is contained in a set, and all of the same type,
namely a cluster cut set. This is to ensure type uniformity which will be important when designing
an automated decomposition method.
==PAGE:22==
12
CHAPTER 3. BACKGROUND
Single/General Cut
The simplest type of cut can be seen in Figure 3.9, indicated with the green line, between the linear
sections t4 and t5. Such cuts are referred to as single or general cuts, and describe a single location
at which a cut can be made. A general cut can be represented by pair or tuple of the two adjacent
sections, between which, the cut should be made. A general cut (x, y) is only valid, if the sections
x and y are neighbours, and if a separation between the two sections would result in two distinct,
disconnected networks.
Cluster Cut
As previously stated, there are certain situations in which performing a cut between two sections will
not yield two disconnected sub-networks. As an example, consider the network shown in Figure 3.9.
In this network, there are several pairs of neighbours for which, performing a cut between them,
would not yield two disconnected networks. E.g. performing a cut with the speciﬁcation (p2, p3)
would still result in one connected component, topologically speaking. That is because p3 would still
be reachable from p2 by following an alternative path and vice versa. In this case, p3 is reachable from
p2 after performing the cut (p2, p3) by following the path:
p2 -> t3 -> p4 -> p3
This is due to the fact, that the sections p2 and p3 exist on the same topological loop which should
not be confused with cycles as deﬁned by the well formed requirement N-06. The di↵erence between
a topological loop and a cycle is shown in Figure 3.10.
Figure 3.10: a) shows a topological loop, b) shows a cycle in a network.
In general, if two sections x and y exist on the same topological loop, it means that y must be
reachable from x (and vice versa) via at least two distinct routes. Therefore, in such cases, cuts must
be performed in multiple places in order to cleanly separate the network into two disconnected pieces.
This introduces the idea of cluster cuts. In essence, a cluster cut is simply a set of general cuts. That
is, multiple general cuts that must be performed in conjunction to cleanly produce two sub-networks.
In the example of Figure 3.9 the valid cluster cut speciﬁcation marked with orange is given by the set:
{{(p2, p3), (t3, p4)}}.
Consider Figure 3.11 which shows how a cluster cut could technically contain any number of
general cuts since the cut speciﬁcation {(p1, p2), (t3, p3), (t12, p4)} would also yield exactly
two distinct sub-networks.
However, as was shown in Section 3.1.2 Point on page 5, problems including points with more than
two branches, can be reduced to problems using only points with exactly two branches. Similarly, there
will always exist a cluster cut speciﬁcation with at most two general cuts, that can cleanly separate a
given network. For instance {{(p0, p1), (p0, t12)}}
==PAGE:23==
3.5. ROUTES
13
Figure 3.11: Network layout with multiple topological loops.
For the purposes of simplicity and type uniformity in design speciﬁcations and implementation, all
functions requiring cut speciﬁcations, either as input or output, will interact with cluster cuts. As
such, a single/general cut can be represented by a cluster cut containing only a single general cut e.g.
( ”t4, ”t5” ) .
Sets of Single Cut
As opposed to cluster cuts, which are sets of general cuts that should be performed in conjunction with
each other in order to cleanly separate a network, for a set of single cuts, it should be possible for the
cut speciﬁcations contained in the set to be performed one after another. Here, every cut speciﬁcation
within the set should yield two sub-networks on a given network and every subsequent cut will be
performed on the sub-network for which the cut speciﬁcation is valid. This means that, if one were
to perform all the cuts speciﬁed in a cut set of size 2, the operations would yield 3 sub-networks. In
general, performing all the cuts in a single cut set of size n will yield n + 1 sub-networks. An example
of this cut can be seen in Figure 3.9, the cluster cut set is the red line in the ﬁgure. As can be seen in
the ﬁgure, this cut speciﬁcation would be given by { { (p1, p2)}, { (t12, p6)} }. The individual
elements of the outer set are also this given by sets { (p1, p2)} and { (t12, p6)}. This is to di↵erentiate
them from cluster cuts while still maintaining type uniformity.
3.5
Routes
As the name suggests, routes, also referred to as interlocking tables, deﬁne the path a train may
take through a network.
Therefore, an additional requirement must be applied to the domain to
ensure safety properties hold when specifying valid cuts. This is because the proof in [HF23] that the
decomposition approach outlined in [MFH17] is sound, is based on the assumption that routes are cut
at most once. An example of a cut violating this assumption is shown on Figure 3.12
Figure 3.12: Train route shown in blue, legal cut shown in green and illegal cut shown in red. From
[HFG23a]
Routes will not be directly applied to networks in this thesis. However, knowledge of routes and their
application is needed in order to ensure the safety properties of a network and for specifying valid cuts.
==PAGE:24==
14
CHAPTER 3. BACKGROUND
3.6
Existing Tool Supporting Network Decomposition
The existing tool used to decompose a network into two sub-networks is made by Anna Nam Anh
Nguyen and Ole Benjamin Eilgaard as a product of their master’s thesis ”Development and Use of a
Tool Supporting Compositional Veriﬁcation of Railway Interlocking Systems”[NE20a]. The tool cre-
ated by Anna and Ole, can given a railway network and a valid cut speciﬁcation, perform the cuts
and return the resulting sub-networks. In addition, the tool ensures that the output networks are well
formed by adding any necessary track sections and marker boards at the location of the cut, so as to
satisfy the boundary conﬁguration requirement.
As seen on Figure 3.8 on page 10, naively performing a cut without any further processing on the
sub-networks can easily result in networks which are not well formed. As discussed earlier, the well-
formedness violations are due to the track sections a↵ected by the cut. Those are, in the case of
the network in Figure 3.8, p1 and p2. In this case, the decomposer tool would append two new linear
sections onto the STEM side of p1 and onto the PLUS side of p2. Marker boards are then placed onto the
newly added linear sections, such that they constitute a well formed border. The ﬁnal sub-networks,
that would result from using the decomposer tool on this network, are shown in Figure 3.13. The
newly added sections and marker boards are marked with lower opacity.
Figure 3.13: The two sub-networks resulting from using the decomposer to perform the cut (p1, p2)
on the network shown on 3.1
When discussing the above mentioned decomposition approach, one might wonder, why it is allowed
to add linear sections and marker boards to the sub-networks, when they did not exist in the original
network. Seemingly, the decomposer tool is adding operational capabilities via marker boards which
were not present in the original network. The rigorous proof as to why these additions are allowed is
presented in [HF23].
==PAGE:25==
3.7. RAISE SPECIFICATION LANGUAGE
15
3.7
RAISE Speciﬁcation Language
This section presents the RAISE Speciﬁcation Language (RSL)[RAI92] which is the chosen speciﬁcation
language for developing the tool. RAISE is an acronym for “Rigorous Approach to Industrial Software
Engineering”[Gro23]. Its intended use is speciﬁcation of software systems.
RSL contains all of the necessary properties needed in order to model and represent a domain. That
includes creation of modules, functions and user deﬁned data structures. Moreover, RSL supports
the functional programming paradigm by being translatable to Standard ML (SML) which itself is a
functional programming language.
Another key feature of RSL, which makes it ideal for the problem at hand, is how closely it resembles
classic mathematical notation. As such, it is relatively simple to express formal requirements while
keeping them legible and eliminating ambiguity.
There are alternative languages that could be considered instead.
Many general-purpose lan-
guages might even have more expressive power than RSL. However, general-purpose languages are
often considerably more verbose than RSL, meaning more lines may be required to express the same
mathematical operation. This can potentially introduce more sources of errors. In short, the step
from formal requirements to executable, testable code is shorter in RSL than in many alternatives.
For instance, RSL has excellent support for set theory, providing convenient operations for union,
intersections, equality, membership and more, as well as value expressions, all of which map closely
to standard mathematical notation. Other languages would require external libraries with the desired
functionality, or overloading various operators. Both of these approaches could introduce unforeseen
behaviour. The former would require trusting that external library behaves as desired, while the latter
could introduce programmer errors if the operators are overloaded incorrectly.
The ﬁnal deciding factor for choosing RSL, is that many of the relevant data types, structures, and
functions have previously been speciﬁed in RSL, making it an ideal choice for further extension. For
the reasons listed above, RSL will in this thesis be used for specifying and designing an algorithm
which will be able to systematically decompose a network into its elementary networks and return any
non-decomposable networks as well.
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 4
Analysis
This chapter serves to analyse the automation of compositional veriﬁcation. As such, the functional
and non-function requirements for the tool will be listed. Furthermore, given the previous work within
the domain, an RSL framework exists.
Therefore, the relevant data types and structures already
deﬁned in the framework will be presented, followed by an identiﬁcation of a suitable algorithm at a
high abstraction level. Lastly, the precise speciﬁcation of the algorithm and discoveries made in the
process will be presented.
4.1
The Problem
As mentioned in Chapter 1 Introduction, the problem to be solved is that of state space explosion,
which ultimately occurs when model checking large railway networks. As such utilising the methods
presented in Section 3.3 Compositional Veriﬁcation and Section 3.4 Decomposition. The missing piece
between bridging the two is then ﬁnding cut speciﬁcations, such that performing them will decompose
an entire network into its elementary and unbreakable sub-networks, as opposed to a human manually
inspecting a network and identifying the cut speciﬁcations. The hypothesis is then that automatically
locating cut speciﬁcations and decomposing a network by those cuts will allow veriﬁcation of large
networks without risking state space explosion.
4.2
Method and Approach
To perform the analysis RSL will be utilised. Not only is RSL quite suitable for working within this
domain as described in Section 3.7. Given the previous work within the domain, an RSL framework
exists. The RSL speciﬁcations made prior to this project have been developed by Linh Hong Vu in
his Ph.D. thesis ” Formal development and veriﬁcation of railway control systems - in the context of
ertms/etcs level 2”, speciﬁed the distilled representation of railway networks as well as the wellformed-
ness requirements. Furthermore, the speciﬁcations have been extended with the decomposition tool
created by Anna Nam Anh Nguyen and Ole Benjamin Eilgaard. Therefore a brief introduction to
these speciﬁcation will be given, along with an overview of the ﬁle structure and the additions to the
framework.
4.3
Functional Requirements
This section introduces the functional requirements of the tool to be developed. As such the tool must
be able to take a representation of a network, to be processed, the tool must report back the found
elementary networks as well as any unbreakable networks as these will have to have further model
checking performed. Furthermore, it should be possible for a user to decide which border the search
is initiated from. Likewise, when no border is given the tool picks one arbitrarily.
16
==PAGE:27==
4.4. NON-FUNCTIONAL REQUIREMENTS
17
These functional requirements are captured in the list below:
BT-1 : The tool must take a well formed network
BT-2 : The tool must return a set of elementary networks and a set of unbreakable networks
BT-3 : The tool must given a borderId start the search with the borderId
BT-4 : The tool must be able to start a search without a borderId if no borderId is speciﬁed.
4.4
Non-Functional Requirements
This section focuses on the non-functional requirements of the tool to be developed. The tool should
be able to run on the robustrails server at DTU and as such the tool must thus be able to execute on
Ubuntu Version 14.04 in connection to that the tool should therefore be a command-line tool. As
mentioned in Section 4.3 Functional Requirements item BT-4.
These non-functional requirements are captured in the list below:
OBT-1 : Must be compatible with the RobustRailS server.
OBT-2 : The tool must have a command line interface.
OBT-3 : It must be possible to pass di↵erent arguments to the tool.
OBT-4 : The tool must be able to output the found networks as XML-ﬁles following a standard
network format.
4.5
Data Types and Structures
Until now the deﬁnitions of the components used to represent railway networks in this domain have
been discussed on a high abstraction level. This section focuses on introducing the most prevalent data
types and structures as they are speciﬁed in RSL, that is rather than listing the speciﬁcations in their
entirety here. As such, comments and functions have been excluded from the speciﬁcations. Relevant
parts of the speciﬁcation will be presented and discussed as they are needed. The full speciﬁcations
can be found in Appendix A. The speciﬁcations have been written by Linh H. Vu in connection to
[Vu15].
For speciﬁcations which are not a product of this thesis, the original author will be credited.
The ﬁrst speciﬁcation to consider is what constitutes the actual railway representation, that is, what
has earlier been referred to as a network is in the speciﬁcation appropriately named NetworkLayout.
The RSL snippet in Figure 4.1 shows, that a NetworkLayout essentially consists of three di↵erent
mappings that each keep track of a type of element, namely linear sections, points and marker boards.
These are all mappings from an identiﬁer (SecId and MbId) to their respective data types (Linear,
Point, and MarkerBoard).
A visual representation of the NetworkLayout type and its relations to associated types is shown in
Figure 4.2. Here, named ﬁelds are represented by components with rounded corners, while types have
sharp corners. In addition, named ﬁelds follow the convention of being speciﬁed in all lowercase, while
types use Pascal casing.
The ﬁelds which have an attached box marked ”key: X ” represent a mapping from the type X to its
connected type e.g. linears represents a mapping from type SecId to type Linear.
Speciﬁcations of the types of the identiﬁers and other common types of the domain have also been
written by Linh H. Vu. These speciﬁcations are written in the ﬁle T.rsl which is available in full in
Appendix A.4. The types of T.rsl are speciﬁed as follows:
Here, it can be seen that the aforementioned identiﬁers SecId and MbId share a common type Id,
which itself is of type Text, a built-in type of RSL representing a string of characters. That is, SecId

Considering the three mappings of NetworkLayout shown in Figure 4.1, it is now clear that they are
mappings from text strings to the respective types Linear, Point, and MarkerBoard.
From the speciﬁcations it is evident that the three types are record deﬁnitions, that is, they are a
collection of named ﬁelds. In the cases of Linear and Point, they are collections of ﬁelds named
neighbors and length. In both cases length is of type Distance and represents the physical length
of its track section. As can be seen in Figure 4.3, Distance is of type Nat which is a primitive type in
RSL representing natural numbers, i.e., unsigned integers.
Perhaps, the more interesting ﬁeld is neighbors which, for both Linear and Point, is a mapping to
the SecId of a neighbouring track section. The main di↵erence between the two mappings is for Point,
the key is of type PointEnd, while for Linear it is of type LinearEnd. As can be seen in Figure 4.3,
LinearEnd is equivalent to the type Direction which can take the values of UP and DOWN. PointEnd
is similar, except it can take one of exactly three values: NB STEM, NB PLUS, and NB MINUS.
Since it is a property of maps, that they can only contain one entry for each unique key, it is clear that
the neighbors map for Linear and Point can have cardinalities of at most two and three, respectively.
This is consistent with the domain since the mappings are meant to model the neighbours of any given
track section and Linear and Point are allowed to have at most two and three neighbours, respectively,
with each entry representing a distinct neighbour.

4.5.1
RSL Framework
Here an overview of the ﬁle structure of the RSL framework is introduced as shown in Figure 4.5. The
ﬁles contained within the blue box are the additions made to the framework as part of this thesis.
A short introduction to each of the ﬁles and their content is given, starting from T and proceeding
down in the Figure 4.5: As mentioned in Section 4.5 Data Types and Structures the T object is the
root of the structure as it does not inherit from any classes. The NetworkLayout and Interlocking
ﬁles have the T object as a nested type. This is shown in the ﬁgure as a circle with a plus inside.
Furthermore, the Interlocking extends from the NetworkLayout. The NetworkLayout ﬁle contains
the representation of a railway network. The interlocking ﬁle contains the route based interlocking
system representation. The ﬁle Decompose AUX contains auxiliary functions for decomposition. The ﬁle
Decompose REQ DEF contains explicit function deﬁnitions for requirements of cut and decomposition.
The ﬁle Decompose EX NOInterlocking was originally called Decompose EX and contained the con-
crete RSL speciﬁcation for the decompose algorithm.
The reason behind creating a new ﬁle called Decompose EX NOInterlocking was to change the param-
eters of decompose to no longer require an interlocking table since they are not explicitly considered.
With that, the precondition to the decompose has been altered such that the following requirement is

An alternative option was considered, where the solution entailed implementing a decompose wrapper

==PAGE:32==
22
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
4.6
Identifying an Algorithm
In order to devise an algorithm that can break apart a network into elementary and non-decomposable
networks. It is worth exploring the previous deﬁnition for dividing networks. In particular, cuts and
the properties of elementary networks are of interest. This will give insight as to which direction to
take the algorithm. Furthermore, pseudo-code of a potential solution for the algorithm was supplied
by the supervisors Associate Professor Anne E. Haxthausen and Professor Alessandro Fantechi, which
served as an initial starting point. These can be found in Appendix B. A key insight is the properties
of elementary networks since they guarantee that all safety properties are met.
As a ﬁrst step, the consideration of topological loops will be omitted in the ﬁrst iteration of the algo-
rithm.
The ﬁrst approach is to ﬁnd elementary networks that are part of the network to be veriﬁed. As
explained and shown in Section 3.2 Well Formed Networks. Elementary networks come in two conﬁgu-
rations, one consisting of an arbitrary amount of linears with four marker boards, and one consisting of
exactly one point and 6-9 marker boards. It became evident quite early, that traversal of the network
was necessary in order to identify certain patterns, namely elementary networks. A natural place to
start would be at a border section, due to the fact that border sections only have a single neighbour,
thus providing an initial search direction, while ensuring all unexplored sections lie ahead of the initial
starting point. As a border can only be linear, its neighbour is required by the 3.1.5 item 3.1.5 to also
be linear. There are now two possibilities when proceeding with the search. The ﬁrst is an arbitrary
amount of linears followed by the required marker boards, in which case an elementary network has
been found.
The second option is the discovery of a point. Since a point can be entered from three point-ends
the following combinations, for possible further explorations are:
1. Entry from STEM - search continues via PLUS and MINUS ends in parallel
2. Entry from PLUS - search continues via STEM and MINUS ends
3. Entry from MINUS - search continues via STEM and PLUS ends
Figure 4.6: The di↵erent search directions visualised on a point, the red dot indicates which point end
the search starts from.
If entry of a point happens from the point-end STEM, the direction of exploration stays the same.
However, the search branches out into both PLUS and MINUS as shown in Figure 4.6 a), note the red
dot signiﬁes the point of entry. In the case of entering the point as in item 2 or 3 in the above list, the
search will expand as well into both point-ends but an important detail is that the direction changes
in order to explore the opposite branch as shown in Figure 4.6 b) and c).
In example 4.6 c) upon entering a point from PLUS in the DOWN direction the exploration of the network
will continue in the DOWN direction for the point-end STEM, whereas the exploration of the point-end
MINUS will be in the UP direction.
Since topological loops are yet to be considered. It is fair to assume, that a point-end can only have
two conﬁgurations for each of the point-ends.
==PAGE:33==
4.6. IDENTIFYING AN ALGORITHM
23
1. The point-end is connected to a series of linears, and eventually a border is reached.
2. The point-end is connected to an arbitrary number of linears before another point is reached.
From the above list, the following scenarios for point-ends are possible, that is:
1. Both point-ends can both terminate with a border.
2. Both point-ends can both expand into points.
3. Lastly one point-end can reach a border and the other a point.
Inspecting the scenario of item 1 in the above list, the search for that speciﬁc point-end can be
terminated, as reaching a border guarantees that everything on the path in question has been explored.
However, in the case of item 2 in the above list. A cut must be performed, as an elementary network
cannot have more than one point as described in 3.1.5. When a cut is to be performed, the decompose
method must be applied as this will result in two networks, one of which is elementary and the other
network being the remaining part of the initial network after separating the elementary network.
Applying the decompose method at the given cut speciﬁcation will maintain the wellformedness of the
networks.
As to systematically keep track of the found elementary networks and the remaining network to
be explored. A general convention will be used. Such that found elementary networks will be stored
on the left-hand side while the right-hand side will store what is yet to be explored. This is to have a
collection of the found elementary networks and a systematic way of keeping track of what is yet to
be explored, that is the original network, with the removed elementary networks.
So far, the possibility that topological loops can exist in a network has been ignored. This possibility
will now again be considered. As was mentioned in Section 3.4.1 , topological loops introduce the need
for cluster cuts. As such, the algorithm which automatically ﬁnds cut speciﬁcations must be able to
identify whether and where cluster cuts are needed. In other words, the algorithm must also be able
to ﬁnd topological loops.
In order to automate the process of ﬁnding topological loops, it is helpful to deﬁne what a topological
loop is and identify the conditions that must be met for one to exist in a given network. An arbitrary
path through a network can be deﬁned as a topological loop if, and only if, the path starts and ends
at the same track section without passing through any intermediate sections more than once, e.g.
back-tracking is not allowed.
Perhaps, an obvious condition for this to be true, is that a network with topological loops must
contain points, since a network containing only linear sections would e↵ectively model a line. Thereby,
it would not be possible to traverse the network from a starting section and return to it without
backtracking, unless cycles are allowed as deﬁned in 3.1.5. However, this is not permitted by the
wellformedness requirements.
In fact, a topological loop must involve at least two points. This is because, if there is only one
point, then following any path from the point outwards would necessarily end at a border section. This
is unless two of the branches are connected via only linear sections as shown in Figure 4.7. However, a
conﬁguration like that would violate the orientation consistency requirement as deﬁned in 3.1.5 since
a train travelling through p1 in the UP direction from the STEM through a branch, would eventually
ﬁnd itself travelling in the opposite direction from the opposite branch through the STEM.
Figure 4.7: Network with only one point where two branches are connected.
In any case, what has become apparent, is that a cluster cut must be performed if there is a topo-
logical loop ahead in the search, and a loop is only possible if the search encounters a point. The
algorithm must take this into account and, therefore, an additional check must be performed whenever
a point is encountered which assesses whether said point is topologically connected to itself via the
==PAGE:34==
24
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
not-yet-searched point ends. E.g. if the algorithm encounters a point from its STEM side, it must assess
whether the point is connected via its PLUS and MINUS branches etc. If the point ends are connected,
the algorithm must return the following cut speciﬁcations as a cluster cut. Otherwise, the cuts should
be returned separately as single/general cuts.
All of the above observations will now be applied in order to write precise speciﬁcations outlining the
algorithm. These speciﬁcations and the process of producing them will be presented in the following
section.
==PAGE:35==
4.7. THE ALGORITHM
25
4.7
The Algorithm
Now that the desired behaviour of the overall algorithm has been described, a more precise speciﬁcation
can be outlined. As may have become evident via the description above, there are two main aspects to
the algorithm. Firstly, there is an actual graph search taking place which identiﬁes where cuts should
be performed on a given network. Secondly, the algorithm uses the decomposer to perform a cut given
by the newly found cut speciﬁcations. Of the resulting sub-networks, one must be elementary and is
stored, while the other represents the rest of the network which is yet to be searched. The algorithm
then repeatedly initiates a graph search and performs new cuts, until no more cut speciﬁcations can
be found, which means all found sub-networks must either be elementary or unbreakable. As such, it
was deemed suitable, to split the algorithm into two main components.
The ﬁrst component will perform the graph searches to ﬁnd cuts, and will therefore be referred to as
the cut ﬁnder. The second component will handle the rest, that is, it instructs the cut ﬁnder when and
where to search for new cuts, uses the decomposer with said cut speciﬁcations, and ﬁnally stores the
resulting elementary and unbreakable networks and ultimately returns them when all searches are over.
As an added aid to the reader, ﬂowcharts for each of these components are presented later in the
section on page 31 Figure 4.14 and on page 43 Figure 4.36.
4.7.1
The Cut Finder
In this section, the precise speciﬁcations of CutFinder.rsl will be presented and explored, as the name
might suggest, the responsibility of the CutFinder.rsl is to automatically ﬁnd the cut speciﬁcations
that are to be used to break a given network into its elementary sub-networks. Each function will be
presented as it becomes relevant. The full speciﬁcation of CutFinder.rsl can been found in Appendix
A.1.
The CutFinder is speciﬁed functionally, meaning it only utilises pure function and has no global state
or values to survey. As a result, the cut search is speciﬁed using recursion with a series of functions
responsible for di↵erent scenarios that might be encountered during a search. Since these functions are
highly inter-dependent, it may be diﬃcult to explain their individual responsibilities without observing
them as a whole. As such, an abstract view of these functions can be seen by the call graph shown in
Figure 4.8.
Figure 4.8: Call graph of the most important functions of the cut ﬁnder, with arrows showcasing which
functions are called by which.
==PAGE:36==
26
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
The ﬁrst action the algorithm performs is, it initiates a search on a network from a given border to
ﬁnd its ﬁrst cut speciﬁcation. This is done via the find cuts function, the speciﬁcation of which can
be seen in Figure 4.9.
1
ﬁnd cuts : I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥T.SecId ! ClusterCut-set
2
ﬁnd cuts (net, border id) ⌘
3
let linear = I.L. linears (net)(border id) in
4
let
5
dir = hd (dom I.L.neighbors(linear)),
6
next to border = next from linear(I.L. get linear (border id, net), dir)
7
in
8
ﬁnd cuts from linear (next to border, dir, net)
9
end
10
end
11
pre I.L.is wf(net) ^ I.L.is boundary(border id, net)
Figure 4.9: Speciﬁcation of the function find cuts.
As shown in the speciﬁcation, find cuts takes two arguments on line 3, that is, a NetworkLayout given
by net and a SecId given by border id. The pre-conditions require, that the given network is well
formed, and that border id is, in fact, a border in the given network. Granted that the pre-conditions
are satisﬁed, the ﬁrst thing the function does is fetch the Linear object given by the border id. As
shown on line 5 dir = hd (dom I.L.neighbors(linear)) in the body of let expression stores the
search direction in dir. This works because dom I.L.neighbors(linear) gets the set of keys in the
neighbors map of the current linear, i.e. the set of directions in which linear has neighbours. This
set will contain exactly one value, since linear is a border as required by the pre-condition. Therefore,
using hd operation on this set will yield the one valid search direction.
The function then fetches the one immediate neighbour and continues the search from there by calling
the find cuts from linear function as shown on line 8. This is done, so that the ﬁrst two marker
boards, which are required due to the boundary conﬁguration, are not considered when making a cut.
find cuts from linear as the name suggests, performs the actions necessary to continue a given
search from a linear section. As shown on the call graph in Figure 4.8, find cut from linear has
four outgoing arrows. That is, from an arbitrary position, it may be able to ﬁnish the search, make a
recursive call to itself, or invoke either find cuts from stem or find cuts from branch.
It is one of the ﬁve functions that handle the overall search for a cut speciﬁcation.
These ﬁve are appropriately named and will be presented in the listed order:
• find cut from linear
• find cuts from stem
• find cuts from branch
• find cuts from side aux
• find cuts from linear2
Each of these functions is given a SecId and other instructions on how to continue the search. That
is, based on its given parameters, the individual function will either terminate the search or fetch the
appropriate neighbour of its given SecId and call the appropriate function with ID of the neighbour
as parameter. Note, that under certain conditions, multiple function calls may be made in parallel,
which will be discussed when relevant.
==PAGE:37==
4.7. THE ALGORITHM

On line 3 of the function it fetches the SecId of the next track section to be explored and stores it in
the variable next id. The function then checks whether the track section given by next id is linear.
In case it is linear, there are three further options. If next id is a border, which is checked using the
function is boundary, the search cannot continue, and no cut has to be made, in which case, an empty
cut speciﬁcation is returned. Otherwise, if either of the considered track sections has a marker board
pointing towards the other, a cut can be performed between the two track sections. This would result
in an elementary network of the type shown in Figure 3.7 a) or b) consisting only of linear sections
and marker boards. If neither of these cases is true, the function simply calls itself recursively with
next id as the new SecId parameter, thereby continuing the search.
The last part of the function to consider is what happens if the initial is linear(next id, net)
check fails.
This must mean that next id is a point and the search must continue using either
find cuts from stem or find cuts from branch.
4.7.2
ﬁnd cuts from stem & ﬁnd cuts from branch
Whether find cuts from stem or find cuts from branch is called and what parameters are used
depends on which point-end next id was entered from. For instance, if lid is neighbour with next id
via the STEM end, find cuts from stem is called. Otherwise find cuts from branch is called, with the
corresponding branch value, i.e. MINUS or PLUS. These scenarios are depicted in Figure 4.6. Together,
these two functions outline the behaviour related to when the ﬁrst point is encountered, meaning the
elementary network, which is being identiﬁed, must be of the type seen in Figure 3.7 c) or d). The
precise speciﬁcations of the functions are shown in Figure 4.11.
The fact that both functions handle point sections can also be seen by their pre-conditions shown on
line 15 and 33 respectively: pre is point(point id, net)

The two functions deﬁned at respectively line 1 and 17 have quite a few similarities. Both of them
have an is connected check, and both of them contain a let-expression which stores two cut values
as found by: find cuts from side aux
Interestingly, the expressions, that the functions have in common occur in di↵erent order. In the case
of find cuts from stem, whether the is connected check is true or false a↵ects how the found cut
speciﬁcations are returned. If is connected is true, the functions should return a cluster cut as de-
scribed in 3.4.1. This is why, the union is taken of the cuts found by searching in each of the branches
as shown on line 7. Conversely, is connected is false, the found cut speciﬁcations are returned sepa-
rately as shown on line 11.
For instance, let cut plus be (a, b) and cut minus be (c, d). If the point given by the identiﬁer
point id is connected between its plus and minus ends, the return value of the function becomes
{{a, b), (c, d)}}. Otherwise, the return value is {{a, b)}, {c, d)}}. The di↵erence is, that the former
value represents a cluster cut as deﬁned in Section 3.4.1, meaning both of the cuts speciﬁed must be
performed in conjunction in order to cleanly separate the corresponding network into two sub-networks.
The latter value represents two general cuts that can be performed separately, and each of the cuts will
==PAGE:39==
4.7. THE ALGORITHM
29
result in two sub-networks. Therefore, performing both general cuts in sequence will result in three
total sub-networks.
In any case, the resulting cut speciﬁcations are based on the ﬁndings of the function
find cuts from side aux.

The responsibility of find cuts from side aux is to take the point id and the PointEnd from which

On line 3 the function fetches the neighbour of the given point at the given PointEnd. If the
neighbor id represents a Point, it means that the search has encountered its second Point which
should not be included, since elementary networks allow at most one point. As such, the cut must be
made between the two points as given by the return value (point id, neighbor id). On the other
hand, if the neighbor id represents a linear section, the search continues and is handed o↵to the
function find cuts from linear2.

On line 4 that is, in the case where next id is linear, the subsequent checks are the same in both

already been encountered once. Therefore, if next id is, in fact, a point, the cut should be made right
before the point as shown on line 12 in Figure 4.13, i.e. {(lid, next id)} in order to ensure that the
resulting network will be elementary. While the call relationships of the functions have been shown
on the call graph, a more speciﬁc behaviour of the cut ﬁnding process can be seen by the ﬂowchart in

but can be seen in Figure 4.15

is connected
The is connected algorithm determines for a given point whether a topological loop exists between
two given point ends. As mentioned previously, it is used to determine whether a cluster cut should
be made, as opposed to single/general cuts. The speciﬁcations of is connected are shown on 4.16.
1
is connected : T.SecId ⇥T.PointEnd ⇥T.PointEnd ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout
⇠
! Bool
2
is connected(pid, point end1, point end2, net) ⌘
3
let target id = next from point(I.L.get point(pid, net), point end1),
4
next id = next from point(I.L.get point(pid, net), point end2)
5
in
6
is connected aux section( target id , next id, pid, net, {pid})
7

pre is point(pid, net)
Figure 4.16: Speciﬁcations of the function is connected.
The algorithm works by ﬁrst fetching the neighbours at each point end for the point in question at
lines 3-4. One of the neighbours is designated as the target ID, while the other neighbour becomes the
==PAGE:43==
4.7. THE ALGORITHM
33
next ID from which the search starts. The algorithm then traverses the network from the current ID,
and if it is able to reach the target ID, the point is found to be connected between the two ends, and
the algorithm evaluates true. In any other case, it evaluates false. The algorithm maintains the set of
section IDs that have been visited so far, such that it may terminate a search if it reaches a section
that has been previously visited, this can be seen by the last parameter on line 6. The ID of the initial
point is added to the visited set immediately so the search cannot ﬁnd a connection to the target ID
via the initial point as shown in Figure 4.17.
Figure 4.17: Undesirable search path for is connected if the starting point is not immediately included
in the set of visited sections.
Once the target and next IDs have been determined, the search is handed o↵to the function
is connected aux section which, in turn, invokes one of the functions is connected aux linear or
is connected aux point depending on whether the given track section is linear or a point. This can
be seen on the speciﬁcations of is connected aux section in Figure 4.18.
1
is connected aux section : T.SecId ⇥T.SecId ⇥T.SecId ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥T.SecId-set
⇠

Figure 4.18: Speciﬁcations of the function is connected aux section.
Meanwhile, the overall relationships between is connected and its auxiliary functions are shown on
the call graph in Figure 4.19.
When the search is handed o↵to the auxiliary functions, the target id gets the identiﬁer original id
and the next id gets the identiﬁer curr id. The ﬁrst thing each of the functions is connected aux linear
and is connected aux point do, is to check whether the search has reached the original id, mean-
ing the search is complete. This is done by checking whether original id and curr id are equal.
The speciﬁcations of is connected aux linear and is connected aux point are shown on Figures
4.20 and 4.21 respectively.
The functions work as a repeating disjunction that for each newly visited section compares the current
ID with the target ID. If they are not equal, the functions will continue recursively checking the next
reachable sections, if any. As such, the operations of the functions will resemble the following formula:
original id = curr id1 _ (original id = curr id2 _ (... _ (original id = curr idi _ ...)))
(4.1)
Where curr idi represents the ID of the section that has been reached at step i in the is connected
search. If at any point, the current and target IDs are equal, i.e. the point ends are connected, the
function returns true, meaning the disjunction will short circuit and evaluate true, thereby terminating
the rest of the search.

Types
In addition to the existing types that make up NetworkLayouts and ClusterCuts, the type Branch
is introduced. Its only valid values are BR PLUS and BR MINUS which are used to represent the PLUS
and MINUS branch of a point, respectively. The reason an existing type, such as PointEnd was not
used is to ensure that the STEM of a point is summarily excluded as a permitted input value to the
function opposite branch. Notably, the return type of opposite branch is PointEnd even though it
will never return NB STEM.
==PAGE:46==
36
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
4.7.5
Auxiliary Functions of the CutFinder
Most auxiliary functions have not been directly described yet.
Their naming is intended to make
their purpose self-evident, such that a reader does not necessarily need to be familiar with the inner
workings of these functions, in order to understand the algorithm as a whole. However, some names
communicate their functions purpose better than others, and in any case, it may be helpful to have
access to explicit explanations of each function. This section will explain and show the speciﬁcations
of all auxiliary functions which are yet to be described.
is linear
The speciﬁcations of is linear are shown in Figure 4.22.
1
is linear
: T.SecId ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout ! Bool
2
is linear (sid, net) ⌘
sid 2 (dom I.L.linears(net)) ^ ⇠(sid 2 (dom I.L.points(net)))
Figure 4.22: Speciﬁcations of the function is linear.
As the name suggests, the function is linear assesses whether a given SecId sid represents a linear
section in the given NetworkLayout net. This is done by checking whether sid is present in the map
of linears in the network and, conversely, checking that sid is not present in the map of points.
is point
The speciﬁcations of the function is point are shown in Figure 4.23.
1
is point : T.SecId ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout ! Bool
2
is point (pid, net) ⌘
⇠is linear (pid, net)
Figure 4.23: Speciﬁcations of the function is point.
The function is the opposite of is linear in that, it assesses whether the given SecId represents a
point section in the given NetworkLayout. In fact, the function is the exact inverse since it literally
evaluates the negation of is linear with the given values. This is possible because the domain only
has the two types of sections, meaning that if an existing SecId does not represent a linear section, it
must necessarily represent a point.
next from linear
The speciﬁcations of next from linear are shown in Figure 4.24.
1
next from linear : I.L.Linear ⇥T.Direction
⇠
! T.SecId
2
next from linear(l , dir) ⌘
I.L.neighbors(l)(dir)
3
pre dir 2 dom I.L.neighbors(l)
Figure 4.24: Speciﬁcations of the function next from linear.
As the name suggests, the function next from linear ﬁnds the next section from a given linear section.
More speciﬁcally, the function returns the SecId of the section that neighbours the given Linear in
the given Direction.
The function takes a Linear as an argument rather than a SecId in order to ensure that the given
section cannot be a point. Alternatively, the function could take SecId instead, and the type could be
enforced through a pre-condition e.g. is linear(sec id, dir).
==PAGE:47==
4.7. THE ALGORITHM
37

The speciﬁcations of next from point are shown in Figure 4.25.

3
pre point end 2 dom I.L.neighbors(point)
Figure 4.25: Speciﬁcations of the function next from point.
As the name suggests next from point ﬁnds the next section from a given point and is very similar
to next from linear just using di↵erent input types. That is, the function returns the SecId of the
section that neighbours the given Point via the given PointEnd.
Again, the type of section, which the function allows, is enforced via the parameter type Point. In
a similar fashion to next from linear, the type could instead have been enforced via a pre-condition
such as is linear(sec id, point end).
opposite direction
The function opposite direction is quite simple and does exactly what its name implies. That is, it
takes a single parameter of the type Direction and returns the opposite. Since Directions can only
take two values, it returns UP if given a DOWN value and vice versa. The speciﬁcations of the function
can be seen in Figure 4.26. It should be noted, that a version of this function has previously been
speciﬁed as - in the ﬁle T.rsl.
1
opposite direction : T.Direction ! T.Direction
2
opposite direction (d) ⌘
3
case d of
4
T.UP ! T.DOWN,
5
T.DOWN ! T.UP
6
end
Figure 4.26: Speciﬁcations of the function opposite direction.
is connected direction
The speciﬁcations of is connected direction are shown in Figure 4.27.
1
is connected direction : T.SecId ⇥T.SecId ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout ! T.Direction
2
is connected direction (curr id , prev id, net) ⌘
3
let linear = I.L. get linear (curr id , net) in
4
opposite direction (hd
5
{direction | direction : T.Direction • direction 2 dom I.L.neighbors(linear) ^ I.L.neighbors(

6
)
7

8
pre is linear (curr id , net) ^ I.L.are neighbors(curr id , prev id, net)
Figure 4.27: Speciﬁcations of the function is connected direction.
This function aids in search related to is connected. Actually, it is only referenced once, namely, in
is connected aux linear where it is used to determine which direction the search should continue in
==PAGE:48==
38
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
given the current and previous IDs. On a high level, this is somewhat trivial to determine; when given
an ID and the previous ID of the search, one should continue in the direction that was followed to get
to the current ID from the previous one. All of this is done in a single line in RSL as can be seen on
line 4, but it may be diﬃcult to discern what it does at a glance. Therefore, it may be helpful to take
a look at the innermost set comprehension and work outwards from there. The partial line:
{direction | direction : T.Direction :- direction isin dom I.L.neighbors(linear) /\
I.L.neighbors(linear)(direction) = prev_id}
Assembles the set of all directions which are in the domain of neighbours of the given linear section,
and for which prev id is the neighbour. Since Direction can only take one of the two values UP and
DOWN and linear sections must have distinct neighbours as per the linear wellformedness constraintsN-
01 3.1.5 on page 7, this set will contain exactly one value. As such, one can safely take the head
of the set and be sure what value is returned. Speciﬁcally, it will contain the Direction one should
follow, if they wished to backtrack to prev id. This means, if the search should continue, it should
be in the opposite direction, hence why the entire statement is passed as an argument to the function
opposite direction.
opposite branch
The function opposite branch is also quite simple, and resembles opposite direction, syntactically,
as its function body only contains a case statement which returns the opposite value of its input
parameter. The speciﬁcations can be seen in Figure 4.28.

It is worth noting the typing of the function. As opposed to opposite direction, the input and
