in this function as described in Section 4.7.4. In short, Branch represents the same possible value as
PointEnd just excluding NB STEM. The return type is still PointEnd such that it is compatible with
other functions which deal with points and their sides.

This information is used in find cut from linear, when the next section is a point, to determine
whether the search should continue using find cuts from stem or find cuts from branch. It is also
used in is connected aux point as mentioned in Section 4.7.4.
The Break Algorithm
As mentioned before, the break algorithm is the main product of this thesis.
It can take any
NetworkLayout, granted it is well formed and returns sets of sub-networks this can be achieved by
breaking apart the given network. These sub-networks must either be elementary or impossible to
decompose any further.
That is, the algorithm traverses the given network and ﬁnds cut speciﬁcations using the CutFinder,
thereby ensuring that when decompose is performed, one of the resulting networks is elementary, while
the search continues on the other resulting network. A visual example of what the break algorithm
produces can be seen in Figure 4.31.
More speciﬁcally, the output is a pair of sets wherein the ﬁrst set only contains distinct sub-networks
of the input networks, which have been found to be elementary. The second set contains all other sub-
networks, i.e. networks that are neither elementary or able to be broken into smaller networks. The
networks are referred to as unbreakable networks. As of writing, the algorithm has never yielded any
unbreakable networks as a result of any of the tested input networks. Neither has an example of an
unbreakable network been found and it is unknown whether such networks can even be achieved using
the break algorithm.
4.7.6
RSL Speciﬁcations of the Break Algorithm
In this section, the speciﬁcations of the Break Algorithm will be presented and explained. The main
break function will be presented ﬁrst, and other signiﬁcant functions will be presented as they become
relevant. Minor auxiliary functions will be discussed last.
An overview of the most signiﬁcant functions and their invocations of each other is provided in Figure
4.31.
The function break is, of course, the main function that initiates the total decomposition of a given
network.
As can be seen by the speciﬁcations shown in Figure 4.33, has a NetworkLayout as its only input
parameter, meaning it needs no external information to successfully break the network apart, as long
as it is well formed which is enforced via the pre-condition.
==PAGE:50==
40
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
Figure 4.31: Above: sample input net, below: Resulting sub-networks after break. The marker boards
of the sub-networks are omitted
Figure 4.32: Call graph of the most signiﬁcant functions of the break algorithm, with arrows showcasing
which functions are called by which.
The return value is of type NetworkLayout-set >< NetworkLayout-set, i.e. a pair of network
sets which is consistent with prior explanations of the algorithm.
The left-most network set shall
contain the elementary networks found, while the right set shall contain the non-decomposable ones.
The function collects all IDs of the border sections of the given network and then grabs an arbitrary
border ID among them, from which the search should start. These are then passed as parameters to
the function break aux along with the given network (with the chosen border ID being excluded from
the set of borders).
Since the function chooses a border ID, e↵ectively, at random, a user cannot guarantee which
border is being searched from. For practical purposes, such as testing, it is handy to have the ability

The only notable di↵erence between the two functions is that break from border takes an extra
argument border id of the type SecId representing a border, which is passed on to break aux rather
than getting an arbitrary border.
The function break aux is responsible for coordinating the decomposition process.
That is, it
initiates a search to ﬁnd a cut speciﬁcation using find cuts. Depending on the type of cut being
returned, break aux will either terminate, start a new search from another border, or pass the cut
speciﬁcations to one of the functions shown in Figure 4.32. As can be seen in the Figure, none of
the three functions has a termination condition, but rather invoke break aux recursively, e↵ectively
making break aux a recursive function.
In order to understand which cut speciﬁcations result in which outcomes, the RSL speciﬁcations
of break aux are provided on 4.35 and a ﬂow chart describing the overall behaviour of the breaking
process is shown in Figure 4.36.
When find cuts returns a cut speciﬁcations cutset to break aux, assessments are made based
on the form of cutset to determine how to proceed. Firstly, if cutset contains only an empty cut,
i.e. has the value {{}}, it means that the current network being considered must be elementary and
it can be returned in the left-hand set of the return value i.e. ({N},{}). This is e↵ectively the base
case of the recursive function, allowing it to terminate eventually.
The next check that is performed is whether the found cut speciﬁcation is completely empty, that
is, has the value {}, as opposed to containing the empty set {{}} like above. The return value {} is
only possible if a valid cut cannot be found, and the remaining network is not elementary. This may
simply be due to the chosen starting border, which has been the case for most observed instances. For
this reason, the functions maintains the set of all borders that have not yet been searched from. As
such, in the case that find cuts returns the empty set, it is possible to choose another border from the
maintained border set, and start a new search from there, in which case, the new border is removed
from the set. If, however, the border set is empty, it means that there are no borders left to search
from, and the network has still not been found to be elementary. As such, it must be concluded, that
the remaining network is unbreakable and will therefore be returned contained in the right-hand set
of the return value i.e. ({},{N}).
Conversely, if none of the above happens, and the found cut speciﬁcation actually contains values,
break aux passes the cut speciﬁcations, along with its other parameters, to one of two functions:

If the found cutset has a cardinality of 1, it means that it either represents a cluster cut or a
single cut, i.e. it cannot be a set of two single cuts. This prompts an invocation of the function

two single cuts, in which case, the function two cluster cuts with one single is called. Consider
the former of the two functions ﬁrst, the speciﬁcations of which can be seen in Figure 4.37.
As has just been established, when one cluster cut with one or two cuts is called, the given
ClusterCut-set must represent a cluster cut or a single cut. To determine which is the case, the
ClusterCut value is extracted from the given set and examined. If the size of the ClusterCut is 1,
it represents a single cut which is the simplest case. The existing decomposer is then used to perform
the cut as speciﬁed on the given network. An astute reader may notice, that the decompose function
call is wrapped in another function decompose output in correct order. The existence and use of
this function are explained in Section 4.8.2. Essentially, it either swaps the positions of the returned
sub-networks N1 and N2, or returns them as they are. What ends up being the left-hand network N1
must then be elementary and the value ({N}{}) is given as the ﬁrst argument to pair union, while
the second argument is the recursive call to break aux which gets passed the remaining network N2.
If, on the other hand, the size of the given ClusterCut is not 1, it must represent an actual
cluster cut, and it is passed onto clustercut contains two single cuts the speciﬁcations of which
are shown in Figure 4.38.
Disregarding the if-expression in one cluster cut with one or two cuts, the bodies of the two
functions are quite similar. Since the function decompose was designed to be able to handle both single
and cluster cuts, the cut speciﬁcation can simply be passed along with the network to be decomposed,

Conversely, for clustercut contains two single cuts which handles cluster cuts, must take both of
the newly created borders into account. It does this by ﬁnding the two new borders using get new border from cut
and stores them in the variables b1 and b1’ respectively. One of the new borders b1 is then passed
as a recursive call of break aux, while the other border b1’ is added to the set of yet-to-be-searched
borders bs. This can be seen by the union operator used in the third argument of break aux:
(bs \ I.L.get boundaries(N2)) [ {b10}
as opposed to the corresponding argument in one cluster cut with one or two cuts:
(bs \ I.L.get boundaries(N2))
This explains why the union operator [ is present in only one of the two functions. However,
one might still wonder, why the intersection operator \ is used for both arguments. To understand
the reasoning behind this decision, consider the network shown in Figure 4.40. In this example, it
is evident that searches have been started from the borders b1 and b4 since they are not included
in the set bs. The other set as evaluated by I.L.get boundaries(N2) consists of all borders of the
==PAGE:55==
4.7. THE ALGORITHM
45
Figure 4.39: New borders created from single cut vs. cluster cut.
right-hand sub-network. i.e. the remaining network to be searched. So the two sets can be expressed
as follows:
bs = {”b2”, ”b5”, ”b6”, }
I.L.get boundaries(N2) = {”b4”, ”b5”, ”b6”, ”mid2”}
Figure 4.40: Diagram showcasing intersection of border sets.
As mentioned, the former set maintains the borders of the total network, that have yet to be searched
from, while the latter set consists of all borders in the remaining sub-network N2 after decomposition.
As such, taking the intersection of the two sets will result in the set of borders in the remaining network
which have not yet been searched from. In the case of the example on Figure 4.40, the evaluation
would look as follows:
{”b2”, ”b5”, ”b6”, } \ {”b4”, ”b5”, ”b6”, ”mid2”} = {”b5”, ”b6”}
This makes sense since one of the excluded borders b2 is part of the left-hand network, which has been
deemed elementary and will thus not be part of any subsequent search.
Returning to the speciﬁcations of break aux, there is one case that has yet to be explored. That
is the case where the found cut speciﬁcation is neither empty or has a cardinality of 1. In this case,
the function two cluster cuts with one single is called, the speciﬁcations of which can be seen in
Figure 4.41.
This is the only function that handles the cut type of two single cuts. That is, where the found cut
set has cardinality of 2. As such, the ﬁrst thing, the function does, is extract both cuts and store them
in the variable cut1 and cut2. Then, as with the other functions, decompose is called using cut1 as
its cut speciﬁcation, yielding two sub-networks N1 and N2. As opposed to the instances of decompose
being performed, N1 is not yet elementary since the second cut has to be performed as well, separately.
As such, a second call of decompose is performed on N1 using cut2. This yields sub-sub-networks N11
and N12.

is, therefore, the only one being added to the set of elementary networks.

separate processes, as indicated by a triangle and its two outgoing arrows.

4.8
Auxiliary functions of the Break Algorithm
While a name like break aux might suggest that it is an auxiliary function, it is actually quite a central
function, as was showcased above. Rather, this section will present all the remaining functions, that
are used by the algorithm, which have not yet been presented.
4.8.1
pair union
The function pair union takes the union of two given pairs of NetworkLayout-sets in the manner
speciﬁed in Figure 4.45.
1
pair union : (I.L.NetworkLayout-set ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout-set) ⇥(I.L.NetworkLayout-set ⇥I.L.
NetworkLayout-set) ! (I.L.NetworkLayout-set ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout-set)
2
pair union(( left1 , right1), ( left2 , right2)) ⌘
3
( left1 [ left2 , right1 [ right2)
Figure 4.45: Speciﬁcations of the function pair union.
In some sense, this function is also quite central to the functioning of the whole breaking process,
in that, all recursive calls to break aux are wrapped in pair union. This means, running break on a
network N consisting of m elementary networks, would take the form:
break(N) = break aux(N, ...) = pair union({N1}, break aux(N2, ...))
)
pair union({N1}, pair union({N2}, ...pair union({Ni}, ...pair union(({Nm−1}, {Up−1}), ({Nm}, {Up}))...)...))
)
({N1, N2, ..., Ni, ...Nm}, {U1, U2, ..., Uj, ...Up})
Where Ni is the ith elementary network found and Uj is the jth unbreakable network found. Although,
in practice during testing and experimentation, the set of unbreakable networks has always been empty,
unless certain wellformedness requirements are relaxed regarding the input network.
4.8.2
decompose output in correct order
A signiﬁcant challenge which arose during testing of the RSL speciﬁcations was that of non-determinism.
Certain operations such as hd used on sets are non-deterministic. This is by design since a feature
of sets is that they are unordered and index-free. However, since the specs and tests must ﬁrst be
translated to SML to be run, when the same test ﬁle is run multiple times, the results are identical i.e.
the output of test ﬁles is deterministic, meaning in most cases, it is possible to implement reliable tests.
A counter-example to this is the decompose function. Given a network and a cut speciﬁcation, the
function is supposed to return a pair of sub-networks where the left and right side networks correspond
to the left and right sides of the cut speciﬁcation respectively.
As an example, let the network shown in Figure 4.42 page 46 be N.
Then the sub-networks N1 and N2 are the result of performing the cut
{("t3", "p2")}.
This would be the expected behaviour of the following expression:
let (N1, N2) = decompose(N, {("t3", "p2")}) in ...
end
That is, the ”left” sub-network N1 should represent the region of the original network where the left
section id of the cut speciﬁcation, e.g. "t3" is located. The corresponding should be true for the
”right” sub-network and the right section id of the cut speciﬁcation, e.g. "p2".
==PAGE:59==
4.8. AUXILIARY FUNCTIONS OF THE BREAK ALGORITHM
49
However, the output has been shown to not strictly follow this expectation. That is, for the RSL
expression:
let (N1, N2) = decompose(N, {(left sec id, right sec id)}) in ...
end
A user cannot be certain, that N1 represents the ”left” sub-network or that N2 represents the ”right”
i.e., the values may be swapped around.
This uncertainty must be eliminated since the grouping of elementary and unbreakable networks relies
on the order in which the sub-networks are returned.
Therefore, the wrapper function decompose output in correct order has been introduced. As can
be seen, by the speciﬁcations in Figure 4.46, the input and output have the type:
(I.L.NetworkLayout >< I.L.NetworkLayout) in common. The only di↵erence being, that the func-
tion also takes a SecId border id as argument.
1
decompose output in correct order: (I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout) ⇥T.SecId
⇠
! I.L.
NetworkLayout ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout
2
decompose output in correct order((N1, N2), border id) ⌘
3
if I.L. l exists (border id, N1) then
4
(N1, N2)
5
else
6
(N2, N1)
7
end
8
pre ⇠(I.L. l exists (border id, N1) ^ I.L. l exists (border id, N2)) ^ (I.L. l exists (border id, N1) _
I.L. l exists (border id, N2))
Figure 4.46: Speciﬁcations of the function decompose output in correct order.
The border id being passed to the function should be the one from which the find cuts search was
started. Then the function returns the two networks in order, such that the left-most network is the
one where the given border id exists. A visual representation of this process is shown in Figure 4.47.
Figure 4.47: Diagram showing how the starting search border id is used to ensure correct ordering of
the decompose output.
All functions related to the break algorithm have now been presented.
There are a few more
functions speciﬁed in RBreak.rsl. However, those functions are related to testing and verifying the
validity of the break functions and their output values. These functions will be discussed in Section
4.10.4 Verifying Output Networks.
==PAGE:60==
50
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
4.9
Modiﬁcations to existing decomposer
A modiﬁcation became necessary as the result of a discovery made during experimentation.
Upon running early experiments on the EDL network, a critical error occurred, in that a sub-network
which was produced at runtime was found to not be well formed. The network in question is shown
on Figure 4.48.
Figure 4.48: Diagram show casing marker boards being overridden.
This unexpected behaviour was found to originate in the last step of decomposition in which linear
sections and marker boards are added where needed to enforce wellformedness. That is, the boundary
conﬁguration constraint needs to be enforced after decomposition, i.e. every border must consist of
two linear sections with marker boards pointing towards the other. In order to investigate what is
happening here, it is worth explore how maps work in RSL.
Since a NetworkLayout is entirely composed of three maps representing Linears, Points, and
MarkerBoards, any new additions must involve a map override (†). In RSL † is a binary operator
that takes a map on either side. The mappings on the left-hand side are overridden by the mappings
on the right-hand side. That is, if a key in the right mapping already exists in the left mapping, its
value is replaced by the value given in the right mapping. If the key does not already exist, the new
mapping is simply added to the map. e.g.
[”key1” 7! ”value1”] † [”key1” 7! ”value3”, ”key2” 7! ”value2”] −! [”key1” 7! ”value3”, ”key2” 7! ”value2”]
(4.2)
Such operations are performed after decomposition every time a border is found to not be well
formed. Therefore, fresh IDs are created that will map to the newly generated linear sections and
marker boards. In order to ensure uniqueness of linear IDs, a convention is followed where new IDs
are created based on their neighbor’s ID, and in the case of Points, the PointEnd through which, the
new sections is a neighbor, is included (e.g. a new Linear placed on the STEM side of the point "p1"
will have the ID: "p1 ex stem"). This is more than suﬃcient for the vast majority of cases.
A similar approach is used with regards to marker board keys. Here, the ID of the linear section, on
which the marker board is placed, is featured in the key. e.g. if a marker board is to be placed on the
section "section1", its key will be created as "mb entry section1". While this approach works well
==PAGE:61==
4.10. TESTING (RSL)
51
for sections that, by deﬁnition, each must have a unique ID to begin with, an issue arises for marker
boards in certain speciﬁc conﬁgurations.
Speciﬁcally, the problem arises when decompose results in a network that consists of exactly three
linear sections and no points as shown in Figure 4.48. This is due to the fact, that when given such
a three linear conﬁguration, both neighbours of the middle section are borders. Therefore, the middle
section plays a part in both borders being well formed. I.e. the middle section must have a marker
board at both ends. This is where the key creation approach described above may result in undesired
consequences. Since only one unique key can be generated for marker boards on each section, a marker
board will be overridden if more than one has to be added to the same section. An example of this
can be seen by the operation shown on 4.3.
The resulting network of a similar situation can be seen graphically on Figure 4.48 b), where the
overridden marker board is shown with lower opacity.
[”mb entry t2” 7! MarkerBoard(”t2”, DOWN, LENGTH)]
†[”mb entry t2” 7! MarkerBoard(”section1”, UP, LENGTH)] −!
[”mb entry section1” 7! MarkerBoard(”section1”, UP, LENGTH)]
(4.3)
As such, the boundary conﬁguration of one of the borders will be violated meaning the resulting
network will not be well formed.
The modiﬁcation made to decompose function was to include the direction, in which a signal is
added, to the marker board key, such that the key to marker boards on the same section are distinct
e.g. "mb entry section1UP" and "mb entry section1DOWN". This minor modiﬁcation was able to
completely eliminate the bad behaviour in all cases where it was previously encountered. For similar
cases, the decompose will now produce networks resembling the one in Figure 4.48 c). Of course, the
same risk of collisions, as discussed above regarding section keys, is still present. However, collisions
are highly unlikely and can completely be prevented if proper naming conventions are followed.
4.10
Testing (RSL)
In order to ensure that testing was performed in a reliable manner, a systematic testing strategy has
been conceived.
The testing strategy consists of three main parts. Firstly, it has been essential to achieve complete
expression coverage.
This is, in part, done by systematically each possible branching path of the
program ﬂow and carefully tailoring test cases to execute those paths. In other words, the test cases
must at least cover all the positions expressed by ﬂow charts in Figure 4.14 and 4.36.
Secondly, the test cases should cover di↵erent values of the same types to increase conﬁdence that
the function behaves as expected in various scenarios. For types with a ﬁnite number of values, e.g.
enumerated types, it is possible to set up a test case for each of the values. This has been done wher-
ever it was deemed necessary.
The last part consists of stress testing the implementation by feeding it various massive networks that
are likely to cover all unique cases. The larger the network, the more scenarios the algorithm and
associated functions are required to handle.
XML to RSL parser
As a supporting tool, we were given an existing parser made by Cecilie Lindberg and Mikkel Blach
Hansen, as part of their master thesis ”Formal modelling and veriﬁcation of a centralised railway inter-
locking system using rsl*”[LH21]. Their parser can process a network deﬁned in XML and return the
respective counterpart in RSL axioms. Since the XML parser extracts all of the necessary information
and only outputs it in a di↵erent format than required in the RSL speciﬁcation of RBreak, it was
straight-forward to change the string-formatter that was already implemented to output an RSL ﬁle in
the desired format. Other modiﬁcations included changing the type of the ﬁelds to be of type String
instead of int. Furthermore, the number of ﬂags was changed to take an input ﬁle, that is an XML
==PAGE:62==
52
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
network, and the name of the to-be-generated output ﬁle. The modiﬁed Java code can be seen in
Appendix H. All translated networks can be found in Appendix D.

made to the toString() function of a point is shown. Similar changes must be made to the classes of

4.10.2
Challenges with non-deterministic cases
There is a quirk in the way newly generated sections are named after a decomposition is performed.
That is, the new sections are named after the section positioned on the ”left side” in the cut speci-
==PAGE:67==
4.10. TESTING (RSL)
57
ﬁcation, and a suﬃx is added. As such, making the same cut on the same network twice will yield
sub-networks that are topologically equivalent but may have di↵erent names for newly generated sec-
tions. Therefore, it is not possible to make automatic equivalence checks in RSL e.g.
break_aux(small3, "t1", I.L.get_boundaries(small3) \ {"t1"}) =
break_aux(small3, "t14", I.L.get_boundaries(small3) \ {"t14"})
4.10.3
Testing the case of non-decomposable networks
As previously mentioned, one major goal during testing has been to achieve complete expression
coverage of the speciﬁcation ﬁles.
At the time of writing, no example of a non-decomposable network, which follows all the wellformedness
criteria, has been found. Therefore, it has been a challenge to set up test cases which execute the
expressions related to identifying and storing unbreakable networks. An example of an unbreakable
network is provided in the article [HFG+23b] which can be seen in Figure 4.55.
Figure 4.55: Example of non-decomposable network. From [HFG+23b]
However, this network is not strictly well formed, as in, it does not satisfy all wellformedness criteria.
Speciﬁcally, it violates N-05) the orientation is consistent. However, if this requirement is re-
moved for testing purposes, the example network is able to pass the wellformedness pre-condition of
various functions, and it becomes possible to achieve full expression coverage.
Test output for breaking the non-decomposable when the orientation requirement is removed from
is wf in NetworkLayout.rsl can be seen in Figure 4.56:
As can be seen, by the output of the second test case [non decomposable break], the ﬁrst set,
which should contain all found elementary networks, is empty, while the second set, which should
only contain unbreakable network, does, in fact, contain the entire non-decomposable network seen on
Figure 4.55.
4.10.4
Verifying Output Networks
For relatively small networks, it is quite simple to manually inspect the resulting sub-networks of
breaking said network and verify, that they are all elementary. However, as networks get larger and
yield more sub-networks, manual inspection becomes infeasible. As such, it has become apparent, that
an automatic method to verify whether a network is elementary is necessary. As such, the decision
has been made, to specify a function which can evaluate whether given networks are elementary.
In order to specify a function as mentioned above, some requirements of elementary networks have
been formalised. Firstly, since elementary networks are a subset of well formed networks, this is a
relatively simple requirement to check that they are well formed.
==PAGE:68==
58
CHAPTER 4. ANALYSIS
[non_decomposable_wf] true

Figure 4.56: Output of running break on a non-decomposable network where the network is stored in
the right-hand set of the result
Secondly, as is apparent from Figure 3.7 page 9, elementary networks can either consist of only linear
sections, or they can contain a single point. In other words, they can contain at most one point.
The simplest case to consider is where the network contains no points.
In this case, the only
allowed marker boards are those, which contribute to the boundary conﬁguration. This is because,
if any marker board is mounted at any other position between the borders, the network can be cut
at that position and yield smaller networks, meaning the original network cannot be elementary. As
such, it is suﬃcient to check, whether the network contains exactly four marker boards since a network
consisting of only linear sections must have exactly two borders, and if it is well formed, the borders
require two marker boards each.
For elementary networks containing one point, there are more aspects to consider since they may have
up to three optional marker boards. Such a network, must have exactly three borders because a point
has three sides.
As with the linear network, the boundary conﬁguration constraint mandates two
marker boards for each border. This means, that a well formed network which contains exactly one
point, must have at least six marker boards, regardless of other factors. Including the three optional
marker boards, it can be stated, that elementary networks containing a point, must have 6-9 marker
boards, or more speciﬁcally the number of marker boards can be expressed as follows:
marker boards = 6 + optional marker boards
Where optional marker boards is the number of total optional marker boards in the network.
As in explained in Section 3.2, the optional marker boards of an elementary network are those mounted
on the linear sections directly adjacent to the point at the closest linear end, as shown in Figure 3.7.
Thus, it should be possible to count the number of optional marker boards given the only point and,
by extension, its immediate neighbours. In order to capture this notion in the speciﬁcations, a function
has been speciﬁed which, takes a network, the point ID, and one of its neighbour IDs. The function
returns 1 if an optional marker board is mounted on the given neighbour, and 0 otherwise.
The
speciﬁcations of this function, dubbed optional marker board, are shown in Figure 4.57.
Summing the results of this function for each neighbour of a point will be the number of optional
marker boards. As such, the expected number of marker boards for an elementary network with a
==PAGE:69==
4.10. TESTING (RSL)
59
1

Figure 4.57: Speciﬁcations of the function optional marker board
point, can be expressed using the newly speciﬁed function for each neighbour like so:
marker boards = 6 + optional marker board(only point id, stem neighbor, net) +
optional marker board(only point id, plus neighbor, net) +
optional marker board(only point id, minus neighbor, net)
Armed with this information, as well as the other observations made above regarding elementary
networks, it is possible to specify a function which takes a set of networks and evaluates true only if all
the criteria described above hold for each network in the set. This function is named all elementary
and its speciﬁcations are shown in Figure 4.58.
The is wf check does a lot of heavy lifting for this function. Without it, the analysis solely based
on the number of points and marker boards would not work. This is because the location of all marker
boards would have to be checked since the boundary conﬁguration cannot be presumed without check-
ing for wellformedness.
Finally, a function has been speciﬁed which can directly take the output of break as input. That is,
the function above is only meant to assess the set of elementary networks, but of course, break also
reports the set of unbreakable networks. The ideal case would then be that all the found sub-networks
are elementary, i.e. that all elementary evaluates true for the found set of elementary networks,
while the set of unbreakable networks is empty.
This function has been speciﬁed and can be seen in Figure 4.59.
It must be noted, that it is not a failed scenario if the set of unbreakable networks contains one or
more networks. Rather, it simply means, that these networks are not elementary and the algorithm
was not able to decompose them any further, and that they must be veriﬁed separately using model
checking. In other words, if the function all elementary and no unbreakables evaluates true for
a given break result, one can be conﬁdent that the found sub-networks are all elementary, but if it
evaluates false, further processing is required.
This function has been well-suited for testing purposes. Especially, since no example of a well
formed unbreakable network has been found yet.

==PAGE:71==
Chapter 5
Implementation
This chapter explains the approaches, considerations, and other choices made when implementing the
breaker tool.
5.1
Language Selection
This section aims to clarify the thought process of choosing the programming language for the imple-
mentation of the breaker tool and as such three options were apparent to begin with.
1. RSL
2. Any language of choice using the Decomposer binary executable
3. Extension of previous codebase rtttgen V3 withGeneralCutV6 implemented in C++
Based on the three above options on how to implement the tool, each item will be considered by its
pros and cons which will be a deciding factor in which option is chosen.
RSL, as suitable as it might be in devising and testing the algorithm, does come with its own limita-
tions as an XML parser is needed for parsing the networks to the correct format of RSL. Furthermore,
a parser for reversing the results back into XML is needed. Parsing and unparsing the network in and
of itself is not a problem per se. However, the true bottleneck lies within the RSL speciﬁcation having
to be translated into SML in order to execute the tool, every time a new instance is to be run, which
in turn would add an additional overhead to the runtime of the tool.
As part of the initial phase of the project the existing decomposer tool is accessible and as such
experimenting with the binary executable of it on a variety of networks has been possible. Therefore
when it comes to selecting a programming language for implementation, the binary executable makes
it possible to choose any language, as long as it is capable of executing the compiled decomposer tool
at runtime. As convenient as it might be to develop the tool in a programming language such as
Java, which we both have a lot of experience with, this solution was ultimately decided against. The
main reason for disregarding the binary executable is due to the excessive translation of networks to
and from XML, as XML would be the only means of communicating with the decomposer and vice
versa. And the decomposer is needed every time a cut speciﬁcation is found, meaning the parser would
have to be called at least twice per cut, once for the elementary network and once for the remaining
sub-network. Furthermore, choosing a third language such as Java would require implementing all the
types and functions of the domain from scratch which would be rather costly.
The last option in the above list is extending the original code base with the new tool.
As the
original tool already contains the representations of networks, the ability to parse and un-parse net-
works, as well as a framework for testing, this is an attractive option. The main reason for being
hesitant in selecting this option is due to having no prior experience in developing in C++, let alone
using Version 11. Choosing to implement in C++, the additional time used on getting familiar with
61
==PAGE:72==
62
CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION
the language must be accounted for. Other than having to familiarise ourselves with the language and
the fact that C++ 11 is an older version, and therefore ﬁnding the correct documentation might be a
bit more tedious, the arguments for choosing C++ strongly outweigh the arguments against it.
From the above reasoning, the language C++ has been chosen for the implementation.
5.1.1
Changes to the Architecture of Existing Code Base
This section gives insights on the design choices and decisions made to the code base, before adding
the new functionality.
The main objective was to create a new package structure namely breakerOSA. The package will
contain all the code in respect to the breaker tool along with all of the test ﬁles and XML repre-
sentations of the networks. As it is common, that there can only be one main in a program, which
also applies to C++, the project can only contain a single main.cpp. The original placement of the
main.cpp has had to be extracted from decomposersAO such that it is now located in breakerOSA.
Furthermore, changing decomposersAO to be a library, rather than an executable, is needed in order
for it to be included when creating an executable of the breakerOSA. The changes made to the ar-
chitecture of the code base are therefore made in the CMakelists.txt ﬁle belonging to the package
breakerOSA. Additionally, it allows for conﬁguring the testing framework such that the test can be
executed separately.
5.2
From RSL to C++
This section describes the overall approach in translating the RSL speciﬁcation to C++. Furthermore,
a description of the method being used to encourage correct translation will be expanded upon.
As an attempt in lightening the workload of implementing the break algorithm in C++, an exist-
ing RSL to C++ translator tool was explored, which technically should be able to take the RSL
speciﬁcations and convert them into the equivalent in C++. However, some known limitations of the
tool prevented it from being applicable. One such limitation is that the translator is unable to handle
recursion through quantiﬁed expressions which is an integral part of the already existing RSL frame-
work Secondly, when experimenting with translating isolated functions, the output in C++ was not
easily readable. Therefore the translations of RSL to C++ became a manual task. In order to have
a systematic approach to the translation of the RSL speciﬁcation to C++. The following guidelines
were decided on to ease the implementation phase and ensure consistency.
1. Pre-conditions will be captured by an if statement, such that an input violates the pre-condition
an exception will be thrown and an error message will be issued.
2. All sets will be represented by standard library vectors
3. All maps will be represented by standard library maps
4. Nat will be represented by unsigned integers
5. Types deﬁned via union deﬁnitions are represented by enumerated types
6. Comprehensions are translated into loops
In addition to the guidelines, the existing project has class representations of NetworkLayouts, Linears,
Points and the di↵erent cut types etc. This lays a solid foundation for translation.
As a starting point for the translation, class representations of the ﬁles CutFinder.rsl and RBreak.rsl
have been created in C++. The name has remained the same for the CutFinder, however, the RBreak
name has been expanded upon in C++ and is therefore renamed to RecursiveBreak. This was done
to more closely follow the naming conventions of the rest of the project. With the two main classes
created in C++, the respective RSL function declarations were added as comments to the .h ﬁle for
respectively CutFinder and RecursiveBreak. This was done as an additional aid in keeping typing
consistent across the two versions.

Table 5.1: Overview of the most common types in RSL and their C++ counterparts
Table 5.1 shows a list of the most prominent types in RSL mapped to their C++ counterparts.
The ﬁrst items 1-3 in the table intuitively map built-in types from RSL to built-in types of C++.
Items 4-6 have existing class representations in C++.
Item 7 demonstrates how sets in RSL will be of the type vector in C++. The reason for choosing to
continue using vectors is due to the implementation of the decompose function which speciﬁes cluster
cut as a vector. In order to use the decomposer and cuts as already implemented, vectors were chosen
to ensure compatibility with the existing code and to retain consistency throughout the code base. As
vectors do not have the same built-in functions as sets, the set operations such as union and intersec-
tion, and equality will have to be implemented.
Item 8 is the NetworkLayout, which is much the same as C++ with the only di↵erence of the names
having the preﬁx RttTgenDkIxl.
Item 12 speciﬁes the general cut in RSL this is deﬁned as a tuple of two strings whereas in C++ the
GeneralCut class has two ﬁelds which contain the ﬁrst and second ID respectively. Perhaps a more in-
teresting type is item 13 which is that of the cluster cut which in C++ is represented as a vector of the
type pointer to the object GeneralCut. The last item namely number 14 shows how the clustercut-set is
a nested vector in C++. Technically ClusterCut is contained in a class of its own with a ﬁeld gcs being
a vector of general cuts, but for all intents and purposes, ClusterCut is treated a vector of general cuts.

The implementation approach was then as follows: Identify a function which does not rely on any
not-yet-implemented functions, and add its declaration to the header ﬁle. Add the implementation to
the source ﬁle following the guidelines listed above. As such, the ﬁrst function to be implemented was
nextFromLinear.

Figure 5.2: The RSL implementation of next from linear without styling/in ASCII
Some of the translation guidelines can be seen by the di↵erences in the deﬁnition of nextFromLinear
shown in Figure 5.1 and its RSL counterpart shown in Figure 5.2.
As shown by the two functions they each have the same return type, as the T.SecId is a Text in
RSL which is equivalent to a string in C++. Looking at the parameters of the two functions there
are a couple of di↵erences between the two. As with the RSL implementation, the ﬁrst parameter
is a linear. Since a linear in C++ is of the type RttTgenDkIxlLinear, when deﬁning a linear as a
parameter it is done as a pointer to the object. The second parameter is the direction, in RSL the
parameter is of the type T.Direction, however in C++ the struct Neighbors contains two type deﬁ-
nitions, one corresponding to LinearEnd and the other to PointEnd. Therefore it is not enough to just
write Neighbors. It needs the scoped operator :: followed by linear nb t to clarify which Neighbor
type is being used. But just like in RSL, the direction is an enumeration of the values UP and DOWN.
Consider the pre-condition on line 4 in the RSL speciﬁcation 5.2 and line 3 in the C++ deﬁnition
5.1. RSL has the pre-condition as a built-in function, making the check seamless. However, in C++ it
is slightly more involved. Therefore an if statement is utilised with a throw std::invalid argument
in order to simulate the pre-condition of RSL. The following method was created.
1
bool CutFinder::nextFromLinearPrec(RttTgenDkIxlLinear* linear, Neighbors::linear_nb_t dir){
2
std::map<int, string> neighbors = linear->getNeighborOids();
3
return (neighbors.count(dir) > 0);
4
}
In general, for pre-conditions in RSL which are not a conjunction of function calls, but rather an
expression, it was decided to capture their functionality in a function in their C++ translation. This
is the case for the function nextFromLinearPrec which returns true if the linear has a neighbour in the
given direction.
Since C++ allows for custom error messages, it was chosen to include information about the check.
As such, it reports back the ID of the linear and the direction in which it does not have a neighbour.
==PAGE:75==
5.2. FROM RSL TO C++
65
Moving on to the body of the function, it stores the map of the neighbouring IDs in the variable
neighbors. Given the precondition passed, the look-up cannot fail, when returning the neighbor in
the given direction.
This serves as an example of conversion which demonstrates the usage of the overall methodology
and guidelines used when translating from RSL to C++
==PAGE:76==

CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION
5.2.3
Approach
In order to optimise the implementation, multiple techniques and methodologies were considered, such
as behaviour-driven development, sprints, and test-driven development (TDD). As the ﬁnal product
is safety-sensitive, the behaviour-driven development approach was not considered. Ultimately it was
decided that applying a test-driven development approach for the converted RSL speciﬁcations would
not only yield the best result with respect to each function being thoroughly tested once implemented,
but also allow for greater exposure to syntax and methods used in C++ in the starting stages.
In order to succeed in using TDD a testing framework had to be selected. As the code base already
had two di↵erent testing frameworks namely libboost and catch2 adding a third framework seemed
redundant and would only add unnecessary complexity to the code base with another dependency.
Both testing frameworks are well suited for unit testing, however, as a safety precaution, libboost was
chosen since it has more macros and is not limited to only writing unit tests. At the time of choosing
it was thought as an advantage to have an extensive framework available, and the reassurance of not
having to pivot later in the process, should the additional capabilities of libboost be necessary.
Besides the testing framework, it was also decided that mapping the RSL ﬁles to C++ classes would
be a good idea. As this would give the implementation phase some structure as to where the di↵erent
functions should be declared. Therefore it was also decided that any missing functionality from RSL
or functions that had been deﬁned in RSL outside CutFinder.rsl and RBreak.rsl but missing in
the C++ project would all be added to one class namely, Helper.h and Helper.cpp. Since all the
functions would be pure and most used in di↵erent classes of the project, it was decided to make the
Helper class static.
==PAGE:77==
5.2. FROM RSL TO C++
67
5.2.4
Helper
As RSL supported sharing of functions by having everything be extended as shown in Figure 4.5 on
page 21, all previously speciﬁed functions were accessible in the ﬁle RBreak.rsl. However, many of
the auxiliary functions located in NetworkLayout were scattered in di↵erent ﬁles in the C++ project.
For example functions, such as p exists and l exists are both located in the C++ project under
Decomposer AUX. This implies that an object of Decomposer AUX would be generated in many dif-
ferent functions and in both classes of CutFinder and RecursiveBreaker. This was not thought to
be ideal, as, ﬁrstly it would be redundant to instantiate the object, secondly preferable to limit the
dynamic allocation of heap memory.
Therefore a static Helper class was created. As creating it as a static class allows for usage without
having to instantiate an object. Furthermore, the class can be used throughout the entire project, by
merely having to import the Helper.h header ﬁle in order for it to be used. Calling a function of static
Helper can then be done as follows:
Helper::someFunction().
Two other reasons for the helper class were, ﬁrstly, it gave a good introduction to implementation
of simpler functions. The other reason us wanting to be able to encapsulate our work from that of pre-
vious projects, reduce dependencies between the other project and classes, and not import more than
needed. As a continuation on the last point, some of the code in the codebase is copyright protected.
Therefore it was crucial to modify these ﬁles as little as possible, as it would complicate the version
control of the codebase.
The Helper class contains the functions for union and intersect operations, which are normally built-in
functions for sets, but as explained in Section 5.2.1 in Table 5.1 sets will be represented as vectors. As
such functions that can perform unions must be implemented, Figure 5.3 shows the implementation for
union on the type NetworkLayout-set or rather its C++ equivalent, vector<RttTgenDkIxlNetworkLayout*>
The otherwise innocent-looking implementation of union on networks, created quite the speed bump

==PAGE:78==
68
CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION
The ﬁrst step in comparing two networks, is to compare each linear, point and marker board. First,
the two sizes of the linear maps are compared to have an early termination in case they are of di↵erent
sizes. Once it is conﬁrmed that they are of the same size, a single linear is examined from the lhs
network. The id of the single linear is then looked up in the rhs map of linears. If it is not found
the networks are not equal. However in the case that the id is found, a comparison of the linear must
happen, that is that both the lhs and rhs have the same neighbours in their up and down direction.
This is outsourced to the TrackSectionComparator. If both linears have the same neighbours at their
respective UP and DOWN ends, the next linear from the lhs map is examined in the same manner. The
same approach is then applied to each point and marker board in the network.
Furthermore, a generic method for unionVectors has been implemented. The implementation then
covers all other types than that of the NetworkLayout, for example, strings or integers.
This concludes the noteworthy di↵erences between the RSL and C++ implementations regarding
their types and auxiliary functions.
5.2.5
CutFinder
In order to be as close to the original RSL speciﬁcations, it was decided that the C++ implementation
of the breaker tool should also be implemented using recursion. As the main concern in converting
the RSL speciﬁcations into C++ code was maintaining the correctness of the speciﬁcations. However,
using a recursive approach can come with a performance penalty. Had the goal been to create the
most eﬃcient algorithm, the implementation of the breaker tool could then have been implemented
with iterations as they do not need a call stack.
The most noticeable di↵erence between the RSL implementation and the one found in the C++
CutFinder.cpp is the pre-conditions requiring the input network to be well formed (is wf). Here,
the C++ version deviates from the RSL speciﬁcations, in that this particular pre-condition which is
required throughout the speciﬁcations in RSL, is left out in the C++ implementation.
The reason for this deviation is that the well formed function in C++ is a separate tool, as in, a
static model checker tool, developed by Linh H. Vu. This tool takes a set of parameters, one of which
is the path to network to be checked. Since the breaker tool at times can create up to three networks,
each network would have to be parsed to XML, in order to be a valid parameter for the static model
checker. As the main reason for choosing C++ was to limit the parsing and unparsing of networks, it
was deemed infeasible.
Therefore it was thought to be better that the responsibility of checking for wellformedness be given
to the user of the tool. That is, the wellformedness of the input network should be veriﬁed separately
beforehand. Given that the input network is well formed, it should not be possible for decomposition
approach to produce a network which is not well formed.
An alternative solution could therefore involve implementing the wellformedness function as in
RSL, simply taking a network as an input parameter and returning true or false. However, this was
decided against since is wf is no trivial function, and there exist multiple ways for a user to verify
that a network is well formed before use of the breaker tool.
A detail in the C++ codebase actually led to creation of the Branch type in RSL. Where the type used
to represent branches in the CutFinder.rsl was originally PointPos == PLUS | MINUS as deﬁned in
T.rsl.
The assumption was made that it represented the PLUS and MINUS branches of a point.
However when trying to use the same type in C++ as shown in Figure 5.5 it became apparent that
the third value INTER signiﬁed something di↵erent than ﬁrst assumed.
In reality point pos t represents the position of the associated railway switch, i.e. whether the STEM
is currently connected to the PLUS or MINUS branch, or in between the two. Therefore, a new type
was created to only di↵erentiate between branches. The changes were simple to make in the RSL
speciﬁcations as a new type was deﬁned at the top of CutFinder.rsl. A corresponding deﬁnition of a
Branch was then added to the beginning of the CutFinder.h ﬁle.
This concludes the main di↵erences between the speciﬁcations in RSL and the implementation of the
CutFinder class in C++

5.2.6
RecursiveBreak
The di↵erences between RecursiveBreak and speciﬁcations from RBreak.rsl will now be elaborated on
in this section.
A signiﬁcant di↵erence between RecursiveBreak and RBreak, is that of decompose in correct order.
The di↵erence lies in the RSL speciﬁcation of decompose in correct order, which takes the border id
from which the search started. After the network has been decomposed, it ﬁnds the network containing
the starting border, as this is the elementary network, as explained in Section 3.2 Elementary Network,
and orders the output accordingly. The speciﬁcation is shown in Figure 5.6.
In contrast to the RSL speciﬁcation, the function decomposeOutputInCorrectOrder, shown in Figure
5.7 does it a bit di↵erently, as it generates the name of the new border from the cut, which is then
used to determine which network to continue the search from, this can be seen on line 159 where the
borderId is tested as being a border in n1, in which case it builds a tuple with the ﬁrst index being
n2 indicating that n2 is the elementary network, and vice versa if the check fails.
This was a deliberate design choice, as this enables the removal of the borderId for the three functions:
clusterCutContainsTwoSingleCuts,
oneClusterCutWithOneOrTwoCuts, and twoClusterCutsWithOneSingle. The declarations of each of
these functions are shown in Figure 5.8.
Note, the network parameter has been abbreviated from RttTgenDkIxlNetworkLayout to Net, in order
to ﬁt the deﬁnitions on the page.
Comparing these declarations to their counterpart in RSL shown in Figure 5.9, it is clear that the
need for the parameter borderId has been eliminated.
The new approach to ordering the output networks could also work for the RSL version but was
discovered too late to justify making the appropriate changes.
The new classes created as part of the breaker tool and their relations are shown on the class
diagram on Figure 5.10. Aside from the main class, classes that are from previous work are represented
by rectangles only containing their names.

5.2.7
Testing Environment
With the testing framework selected, the testing environment had to be conﬁgured within the project.
Therefore, a test folder was created under the breakerOSA folder and an additional executable had to
be created. The test executable is aptly named breaker test and is added to the CMakeLists.txt.
An outer ﬁle called test runner.cpp orchestrates which test ﬁles should be run. This has allowed for
gaining a better overview in the scenario of test cases failing, and allowed for selected test ﬁles to easily
be omitted when wishing to focus on other test cases. The ﬁrst test that was created was related to
the XML parser. As with the RSL speciﬁcations, the interlocking table is removed as a parameter to
the decompose function since they are not considered by the breaker tool. Therefore it was necessary
to ensure the correct parsing of XML ﬁles, as the interlocking object is intertwined with the parsing
of XML ﬁles.
Being able to parse XML ﬁles of networks is an integral part of testing.
Turning to look at the di↵erent components used for testing, There are three main parts that
constitute the testing environment.
1. BOOST FIXTURE TEST SUITE
2. BOOST AUTO TEST CASE
3. Fixtures
==PAGE:82==
72
CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION
Figure 5.10: Class diagram showing the classes created for the breaker tool
==PAGE:83==
5.2. FROM RSL TO C++
73
The three macros in the above list are what will constitute the testing environment. In the instance
of wanting to parse a network, and use the network among all test cases within the test suit, creating
a suite of type BOOST FIXTURE TEST SUITE is advantageous.
This allows for accessing public and
protected members of a ﬁxture [Roz]. This, in turn, helps with reducing duplicate code when writing
tests.
A template for a test suite can be seen in Figure 5.11. Multiple test suites can exist in the same

test ﬁle, by adding more test suites below line 10 in 5.11, however, test suites must have a unique
name, this counts for all test suites and not just the ones in the same ﬁle. Other test suites can then
utilise the same ﬁxture.
An example of a test ﬁxture that is used frequently throughout the testing of the breaker tool is
that of the InputFixture shown in Figure 5.12. Its purpose is to instantiate an object of the type
InterlockingParser which is used to parse networks speciﬁed in XML to their corresponding C++
representation. The ﬁxture is responsible for instantiating the object as well as deleting the object
once the tests are ﬁnished, that is, when BOOST AUTO TEST SUITE END() has been reached.

Other Fixtures that have been created for testing purposes are networks that were manually cre-
ated, that is, there was no XML ﬁle to be parsed. Even a small network of 5 linears and 8 marker
boards requires 35 lines of code. So, being able to reuse these types of networks proved to be a great
asset. Declaring networks directly in C++ also enabled testing without relying on the parser which,
in turn, made it possible to make comparisons to the results of the parser.
Figure 5.13 shows the structure used. In order to deﬁne global objects within a test suite they must be
placed before the test case that uses them, therefore in the example a global CutFinder is instantiated
on line 2.
Line 5 initialises the expected cluster cut and a general cut is added with the expected values.
On line 8 the actual cluster cut is stored, that is, the cut found by the cutFinder calling findCutFromSideAux.
The result is then evaluated with the macro BOOST REQUIRE. This macro has proven to be very useful
since it is a critical assertion, meaning that if the argument given does not return true it fails, and
had there been another logical check after line 11 it would not have been executed. As such, it be-

using its equality operator =.

.
Comparison of ClusterCuts is a bit more tedious as both ClusterCuts have to be unpacked.
The two functions had to be tested. This was not only in regards to following the TDD approach but
also becuase comparisons between cuts are a crucial building block, in future testing. This is something
that was easily taken for granted, when writing tests in RSL, whereas in C++ an error in either imple-
mentation of isEqualTo could cascade throughout the testing of other functions, ultimately leading
to spending more time debugging than necessary.

5.6
Developement Environment
5.6.1
Docker
Since the original codebase was developed on a Linux system, and neither of us uses Linux as a native
operating system, we decided to containerise the codebase using Docker. This decision is motivated
by the ﬂexibility Docker provides in terms of implementation and execution of the code regardless of
which operating system is being used while minimising the number of errors that can be introduced
when using di↵erent systems for development. A added bonus of having the project containerised
with Docker is that the code can now run on most popular software systems and work as expected,
regardless of the host machine. Furthermore, it could also provide longevity to the codebase in terms
of maintainability and in terms of future development of the software tool.
The ﬁrst task in containerising the decomposer project was to follow the original user guide made
by Anna and Ole [NE20b] and add all the commands to a Dockerﬁle. This forces Docker to install the
necessary libraries, such that all the libraries are installed in the container.
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 6
RSL Experiments & Results
This Chapter elaborates on the types of experiments that have been performed on the implemented
version of the break algorithm in the RSL language. The experiments have been executed on the Ro-
bustRailS server provided by DTU Compute. The hardware that the experiments were performed on
has the following specs: Intel Core i5 CPU 750 (-MPC-) @ 1.20GHz, 16GB RAM, Ubuntu 14.04,
Linux 3.19.0-25-generic x86 64 (64 bit, gcc:
4.8.2) kernel. In order to measure the run-
time of the execution of the algorithm runlim is used. Runlim is a Linux program that can be used
to run and monitor benchmarks under resource limits for time and memory usage.[MH] The primary
feature of runlim used in running the experiments are the timing and memory consumption of the
execution of the break algorithm.
Runlim can take any executable program and associated parameters as argument. Then Runlim does
the necessary set up to monitor time and space consumption during execution of the given executable
and reports the consumption values.
Runlim can be conﬁgured such that the results are stored in two separate ﬁles. The ﬁrst ﬁle contains
the performance benchmark generated by the runlim program, while the second ﬁle contains whatever
is written to the terminal by the given executable. By convention, the naming of the two ﬁles are
as follows. network name border xx performance.result and result network name border xx_log
where the xx for both ﬁles indicates the experiment number on the network. To utilise the Robus-
tRailS server the best, the experiments were performed during a period where we were certain of being
the only users working on it, and as such providing the best circumstances for running the experiments.
6.0.1
Experiment Design
This section presents the design choices made for ensuring equal behaviour for each of the experiments.
As all of the testing so far has been performed on smaller networks that forces the algorithm into all
branches of the code, and these networks purpose serves to control edge cases. Therefore the networks
for which the experiments are to be performed on are larger, with some of them representing railway
networks in the real world. An overview of the networks for which the experiments will be performed
can be seen in Table 6.1 The table contains the names of the networks, the amount of linear sections,
points and marker boards used in order to represent the network, as well as the number of borders in
each network.
In order to get a benchmark for the algorithm, it was decided that for each network, the algorithm
should be called for each of the borders in the given network. To ensure that the results are comparable
with those using the original model checking tool, the network representation must be built each time
the algorithm is to be executed from a new border. This is to also ensure equal overhead for each of
the borders. This is advantageous as it allows for di↵erent traversals of the networks, and for a more
precise benchmark.
For the strategy to be executed as described above, a ﬁle structure which can support the require-
ments had to be created before the experiments could begin.
80
==PAGE:91==
81

The ﬁrst step is being able to represent the network in RSL. By processing the XML-network ﬁles
using the revised/modiﬁed parser for XML to RSL, as described in Section 4.10. Once the network
was parsed a ﬁle containing only the network and a test case with is wf was performed. A single
network, namely rk nv had to be manually inspected and modiﬁed in order to adhere to the is wf
criteria. After controlling that all translated networks were well formed, the wellformedness test case
was removed in order to not meddle in the experiment.
The network ﬁle was then named as shown in Figure 6.1, namely network Experiment, where network
is replaced with the name of the network. By making the ﬁle an extension of RBreak it enables the
relevant functions to be used in all other ﬁles which inherit from network Experiment. The actual
experiment ﬁles follow the naming convention networkname border xx.rsl. The ﬁles contains exactly
one test case which is the break aux with the id of a speciﬁc border. All of the ﬁles used for the
experiments can be found in Appendix C.
Figure 6.1: Class diagram show casing the experiments structure.
An overview of the networks, linears, points, marker boards and borders can be seen in Table 6.1
In total 66 experiments are to be executed, that is calling the algorithm from each of the borders.
Execution of Experiments
Before the experiments can be run the RSL ﬁles have to be translated into SML ﬁles, this was necessary
as not to include the translation time in the experiment. This is done with the following command
rsltc -m "filename.rsl". Which creates an SML ﬁle that can be executed.
In order to run the di↵erent experiments with as few external variables as possible, the runlim
command is executed with the conﬁguration seen in Figure 6.2
==PAGE:92==
82
CHAPTER 6. RSL EXPERIMENTS & RESULTS
./runlim -o performance.result sml <network_name.sml > result.log
Figure 6.2: runlim command for executing experiments on network.
The -o performance.result stores the di↵erent measurements made by the runlim tool, whereas
sml < network name.sml starts the execution of the sml ﬁle. Lastly, > result.log pipes the SML
output to a ﬁle called result.log. For the experiments performed, the result.log ﬁle contains all the
sub-networks found and instantiation of objects done in SML.

This section contains the results of the di↵erent experiments performed on the RobustRailS server.

A brief description of each network is given below, including where they originate:
• lvr 1: Represents a train station in Binche, Belgium. From [FGHL22].

• EDL: The Early Development Line, sometimes referred to as rk nv was the ﬁrst regional line to
be commissioned in The Danish Signalling Programme [MFH16]. The line runs between Roskilde
and Næstved stations in Denmark, hence the alias rk nv.

The table lists the experiment number, the network for which the break algorithm was performed on.
Furthermore, a reference to the experiment ﬁle is added and as a last piece of information, the border
ID used to initiate the algorithm. The following three columns namely Real, Time, Space are from the
testing tool runlim. Where Real indicates the time it took to execute everything, including the runlim
setup. Time is the actual measurement of the algorithm, and Space is the amount of memory used.
Finally, the column Sub-networks, expresses how many elementary networks the original network can
be ”decomposed” into.
Upon inspection of the data the ﬁrst ﬁve networks Real, Time and Space, do not seem to di↵er much
when breaking from each of the borders. But there is a signiﬁcant reduction of the time spent on EDL
which can be seen on row 63 versus the worst time which is recorded on row 54. This is an interesting
discovery, it means that breaking from border: tdt kj kn is 294, 83/64, 29 ⇡4, 5 time faster than the
slowest border.
As the time di↵erence were so signiﬁcantly reduced, it was ﬁrst thought to be an error, but upon
executing the experiment multiple times, and by manual inspection of the sub-networks, it was con-
ﬁrmed that the algorithm did indeed ﬁnish correctly 4, 5 time faster. It raised the question: are some
borders better to break from than others? Why is the same behaviour not noticeable in the other
networks. For example the network SdMext for experiment 17 and 16 there is a di↵erence of approx-
imately 200 milliseconds, this didn’t raise any suspicion as with EDL. But perhaps the only reason
for there not being a bigger gap between some of the experiments is due to the size. Even though
the networks in the experiments are bigger than any test network EDL is still by far the largest network.
A possible explanation of the increase/reduction of time is the is connected checks that happens for
all points, but upon entry from a branch, if is connected evaluates true, a new border is chosen to
search from. This can potentially create a large overhead, when searching for cuts. However in terms
of reducing the time, it is also is connected, but when entry of the point happens from the STEM side,
a cut is made. This is an evident quirk of the algorithm since routes are not to be cut more than once
as explained in Section 3.5. If it would be possible to cut routes more than once, while ensuring the
safety properties of the network, elementary networks would be generated from the find cuts branch,
and the check of is connected would not result in repeated searches.
The average of the three measurements can be seen in Table 6.3.

6.2
Experiment allowing routes to be cut multiple times
As was explained in section 3.5, there is a reason for having two di↵erent functions to handle points,
namely find cuts from stem and find cuts from branch. The reason being, routes may only be cut
once, and if a branch is found to be topologically connected to its stem via an alternate route, a cluster
cut would be necessary to separate the network into two sub-networks. Though, such a cut would
risk cutting potential routes more than twice. Again, this is why the function find cuts from branch
returns an empty cut if is connected evaluates true, thereby prompting the break algorithm to start
another search from a new border.
To reiterate, this behaviour is due to the fact, that the proof of the compositional veriﬁcation approach
is sound, is based on the assumption, that routes are cut at most once per decomposition.
Though, out of curiosity, an experiment has been performed in which cluster cuts found from branches
are allowed. This is to test the idea about a potential cause for the large discrepancy in running time
depending on the starting border. The idea being, that searches starting from certain borders may
encounter more cases where is connected evaluates true when ﬁnding cuts from a branch which, in
turn, would require more searches to be restarted from other borders. This could potentially cause a
signiﬁcant increase in overall runtime.
In order to run this experiment, another version of the CutFinder has been made, aptly named
BadCutFinder, in which, the only changes made were to the function find cuts from branch. The
speciﬁcations of the new version of the function are shown in Figure 6.3.
The new version is made to resemble find cuts from stem, in that, if is connected evaluates true,
a cluster cut is returned rather than an empty one. Aside from this change, the experiment will be
conducted in exactly the same manner as on the original EDL network, i.e. the algorithm is run once
from each border.
==PAGE:96==
86
CHAPTER 6. RSL EXPERIMENTS & RESULTS
1
ﬁnd cuts from branch : T.SecId ⇥T.Direction ⇥Branch ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout ! ClusterCut-set
2
ﬁnd cuts from branch(point id, dir, branch, net) ⌘
3
let cut stem = ﬁnd cuts from side aux(point id, T.NB STEM, dir, net),
4
cut opposite branch = ﬁnd cuts from side aux(point id, opposite branch(branch),
opposite direction(dir), net)
5
in
6
if is connected(point id , T.NB STEM, opposite branch(branch), net)
7
then {cut stem [ cut opposite branch}
8
else
9
if cut stem = {} then {cut opposite branch}
10
elsif cut opposite branch = {} then {cut stem}
11
else {cut stem, cut opposite branch}
12
end
13
end
14
end
15
pre is point(point id , net)
Figure 6.3:
The experimental version of the function find cuts from branch as speciﬁed in
BadCutFinder.rsl
The results of the experiment can be seen in Table 6.5. Here most of the columns represent the same
values as in the original experiment. However, there are two new columns, Time Di↵erence and Space
Di↵erence. These show the relative di↵erence in time and space consumption of the new experiments
compared to the original ones. On average, the experiments using BadCutFinder.rsl ﬁnished 28.5%
faster than the original, but used 2.3% more memory.
Interestingly, the reduction in time usage was not applied uniformly across all borders. The reduction
is seen to range anywhere from negligible at 1.3% to very signiﬁcant at 69.5%. There is even a case,
that of border #2, in which the runtime increased signiﬁcantly at
23.2%. The experiment for this
border was run several times to ensure it was not just a one-time error, and the results were always
within two seconds of each other.
Overall, the changes made to the algorithm did result in measurably faster runtimes. However, the
results do not directly support the idea, that the original discrepancy between starting borders was
caused by find cuts from branch excessively requiring new cut ﬁnding searches to be initiated. If
this were the case, one would expect all runtimes to approach the same minimum value. As such,
the time discrepancy cannot be solely attributed to find cuts from branch. Of course, there can be
many reasons for the time discrepancy at once, especially for such a large network.

Chapter 7
C++ Experiments & Results
This chapter elaborates on the experiments of the implementation done in C++. The experiments like
the ones done for RSL are also performed on the RobustRailS Server. Since the networks will be the
same a similar strategy had to be devised. But instead of having to create multiple ﬁles, a ﬂag -b
was added. In order to implement the option -b, a new function was added called breakFromBorder,
which takes the user input removes the border from the set of borders and calls the function breakAux.
With the new function and ﬂag added. The executable was added to the RobustRailS server. The
command used to execute the experiments with can be seen in Figure 7.1.
./runlim -o ../../experiment_performance/network_name/network_name_border_01.result
./breaker -i ../xml/network_name_OSA.xml -b borderid -p >
../../experiment_output/network_name/network_name_border_01.log
Figure 7.1: Template of the command used to execute the experiments with.
For storing the result two folders were created namely experiment performance and experiment output.
The folder for experiment performance stores the result captured by runlim. Whereas experiment output
stores the printed sub-networks, which allows for manual inspection of the results if deemed necessary.
The above code snippet shows the implementation of the function breakFromBorder. The function
1
tuple<vector<RttTgenDkIxlNetworkLayout*>, vector<RttTgenDkIxlNetworkLayout*>>
2
RecursiveBreak::breakFromBorder(RttTgenDkIxlNetworkLayout* network, string borderId) {
3
auto boundaries = Helper::getBoundaries(network);
4
boundaries.erase(std::remove(boundaries.begin(), boundaries.end(), borderId), boundaries.end());
5
return breakAux(network, borderId, boundaries);
6
}
Figure 7.2: The C++ implementation of breakFromBorder.
breakFromBorder returns a tuple consisting of two vectors, the ﬁrst stores all the elementary networks
and the second vector stores the unbreakable networks. The function parameters are the network
and the borderId from which to start the search. The ﬁrst thing the function does is to fetch all the
borders in the network. Next, it removes the given borderId from the boundaries and ﬁnally it calls
the breakAux function, with the network, the borderId and all other borders.
7.1
Results
In this section, the results of the experiments for the C++ implementation will be presented. Table
7.2 lists the results of the experiments. With a quick glance at the table, it is evident that for the
ﬁrst ﬁve networks, execution time and space usage are at 0. The only measurable metric recorded by
runlim is that of Real, which is for the most part in the range of 100 milliseconds to 500 milliseconds.
I.e. the set-up of runlim takes longer than the execution of the algorithm itself. This was initially
88
==PAGE:99==
7.1. RESULTS
89
thought to be an error, but upon manual inspection of the program output, all the experiments were,
indeed, found to output the expected sub-networks. The measurable result is that of EDL which in
comparison to the RSL implementation is a fraction of the time. This proves that though the networks
were large enough to give some insight into the inner workings of the algorithm, it is clear that the
C++ implementation provides a signiﬁcant performance boost compared to the RSL/SML version. It
also proves that larger networks are needed, in order to actually capture the time and space.

7.2
Exploratory Experiments
In order to get more tangible results, the decision was made to fashion the two largest networks
together to create an even larger network to perform experiments on. This was done by taking the
XML conﬁguration of EDL and ﬁsmn, selecting one border from each network, and fusing them
together by giving them neighbour references to each other. The chosen borders are tdt ol nv for
EDL and T77 for ﬁsmn. A simpliﬁed illustration of the conﬁguration is shown in Figure 7.3.
Experiments have been made by running the algorithm starting from each border. The results are
shown in Table 7.4. The original RT-Tester tool has not been applied to this newly created network,

The average execution time and memory consumption are noticeably higher, as might be expected
of a larger network. More interestingly, perhaps, is the range of the results, depending on starting
border, is signiﬁcantly wider with the minimum and maximum execution times being 0,49 seconds
and 14,69 seconds respectively. Previously, the supposition was made, that the discrepancy in exe-
cution time between di↵erent borders might be caused by the is connected check in the function
find cuts from branch as was explored in the experiments in Section 6.2 Experiment allowing routes
to be cut multiple times.
However, the results of those experiments did not seem to support the supposition. However, this
experiment raised an idea, that the discrepancy in execution time may still indeed be a result of the
function is connected, but rather when it evaluates false. This idea is expanded upon further in
Section 8 Discussion.
In short, the idea is, that in most cases is connected may be more expensive when evaluating false,
since an exhaustive search of the remaining network is needed, in order to ensure that a given point is
not connected.
This might be very relevant for the fused network since they are only connected via their ex-borders sec-
tions tdt ol nv and T77. As such, for any point which is close to the fused borders, their is connected
search enters the opposing network, with no other way of returning which would force a traversal of
the entire opposing network.
This might be especially signiﬁcant for searches starting in the ﬁsmn part since any is connected
search which crosses over to the EDL part of the network, would result in an exhaustive traversal of
a network much larger than ﬁsmn itself. This may well be reﬂected in the result of Table 7.4 since it
can be seen, that the slowest executions taking 10+ seconds all start from borders sections originating
in ﬁsmn i.e the experiments on rows 15-36.
To further explore this notion, a ﬁnal experimental network was made, by fusing a second pair of
borders from EDL and ﬁsmn together, such that is connected searches starting in one network and
reaching the other, could potentially return and evaluate true. The borders were somewhat arbitrarily
chosen, with the only requirement being, that they have same orientation as the previously fused
borders, i.e. both EDL track sections become DOWN neighbours of the ﬁsmn sections. The chosen
border section to be fused, were b kj 0x4 from EDL, and T76 from ﬁsmn. A simpliﬁed illustration of
the new network is shown in Figure 7.4.
The results of running the C++ breaker tool on the new network from each remaining border are shown
in Table 7.5. Again, the fastest starting borders e.g. tdt kj kn remain fast, even though the average
execution time and memory consumption have increased. However, the slowest starting borders of the
previous experiment with only 1 connection between the two networks, seem to have a relatively low
increase in time. Looking at the last four borders of each table, they have gone from taking 15 seconds
to complete to 17 seconds. This may lend some credence to the idea, that the bulk of the execution
time for some borders, is spent exhaustively searching networks when points are not connected.
