Chapter 8
Discussions & Future Work
During the course of writing this thesis and doing the associated work, many interesting ideas were
discovered. Many of these were investigated and the resulting ﬁndings have been included in the re-
port. Many others, however, could not be investigated, as other tasks were prioritised. In this chapter,
some of the interesting ideas which could not be thoroughly investigated will be presented, as well as
potential avenues for future work.
It did seem quite curious, that the same network, can have a such di↵erence in runtime depending on
which starting border is chosen. The biggest di↵erence observed, was a factor of 25 as seen by the results
in Table 7.5 where the minimum execution time is 0,69 seconds while the maximum is 17,39 seconds.
At one point, we hypothesised that this could be due to the fact that when find cuts from branch
is called, and is connected evaluates true, a new search from another border must be started. This
culminated in the experiment using the BadCutFinder as described in Section 6.2.
However, the results of those experiments did not seem to support the supposition. Upon further
reﬂection, another possible cause for the discrepancy has been identiﬁed. Again, is connected was
considered. What we failed to consider previously, is that is connected short circuits its evaluation
only when evaluating true as described in formula 4.1 on page 33. As such, evaluating false is likely
more expensive in most cases since it requires an exhaustive search of the remaining network, in order
to ensure that the point in question is not connected between the considered point-ends. To illustrate
this idea, consider an extreme case, as shown in Figure 8.1.
Where the part of the network depicted to the left of p branching is a long series of connected points,
while none of the points to the right of p branching are connected. Given this network, initiating from
searching from the border b1 would result in many brief is connected searches which would result in
ﬁnding rapidly cluster cuts until reaching the point p branching. After this point, all subsequent points
and their is connected checks will result in an exhaustive traversal of the remaining sub-networks
which could be quite expensive. Consider then, starting from the border b2. Since the number of
points in the network remains the same, this would require just as many calls of is connected. How-
ever, these calls would also require traversing the entire stretch between p branching and b1 for every
Figure 8.1: Extreme example showcasing expensive is connected searches.
96
==PAGE:107==
97
point, until a cut is performed somewhere between the two.
This was partially explored by the experiments in Section 7.2 Exploratory Experiments. The results
could show some indication as to the prevalence of this idea. Since EDL models a train line between
Rosklide and Næstved, the fused network could resemble a less extreme version of the network in
Figure 8.1, and explain one reason, why the fastest borders to break from are original part of EDL.
This could be explored further by graphical inspection of the networks topology.
In any case, a prime candidate for improving the eﬃciency of the algorithm is the is connected
function. One possible way to increase eﬃciency could be to modify the search in a way, such that
it could register other connected points while searching whenever possible. Then the algorithm could
cache the values to be looked up later using the point ID and the two point-ends as a key, which
could eliminate the need for some searches. This would increase the needed memory, but this could
be mitigated by clearing the cache of any point, which has been added to the set of elementary networks.
Another potential improvement to the tool, would be to integrate the static checker, or some other
kind of wellformedness assessment, such that the user need not check for wellformedness independently.
A feature that could be added to the functions used to verify output networks, would be to check
whether the track sections of the output networks are indeed a result of the input network. That is,
that all track sections of the input network must exist in some output network, i.e.
inputSectionIds \ outputSectionIds = inputSectionIds
and that the set di↵erence between the input and output only contains freshly generated sections, i.e.
those with suﬃxes like ” ex stem”, ” ex stem b” etc.
==PAGE:108==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
The goal of this thesis was to identify and specify an algorithm which could support compositional
veriﬁcation of railway networks by automating the process of ﬁnding cut speciﬁcations which, when
performed, would yield sub-networks with certain properties. These sub-networks should either be
of an elementary type, the safety properties of which are known to hold, or otherwise be grouped
separately for an alternative veriﬁcation approach. As such, each sub-network is able to be veriﬁed
separately, and as such the original network is veriﬁed compositionally.
This was to provide an alternative method to verify safety critical features of a railway network
which, until now, has typically been done using model checking which results in state-space explosions
given suﬃciently large networks.
Using the speciﬁcation language RSL, precise behaviour and requirements for the algorithm were able
to be speciﬁed. This led to the creation of a tool which was able to execute the algorithm on any
given network. This allowed the algorithm and its associated functions to be tested in all manner
of ways which, in turn, illuminated unexpected behaviour that was then able to be rectiﬁed. This
provided assurance as to the correctness of the algorithm and ultimately served as a precise blueprint
for implementation in the more performance-oriented language C++. As such, the same tool was
implemented in C++, following a strict translation strategy.
The ﬁnal tool was, via testing and
experimentation, found to behave as expected and provided a signiﬁcant performance boost in relation
to its RSL counterpart. Therefore, the goals of the thesis have been achieved as a highly performant
tool supporting compositional veriﬁcation has successfully been developed and is ready for future use.

B.2

else -- no signals between l and n
20
findcutfromlinear(n, dir)
21
end
22

23
case

25

26

27
end -- case
28
end -- if
29
end

-- input: l is a linear section, dir is search direction
34
-- output: a cluster cut containing 0 or 1 single cut
35
findcutfromlinear2(l, dir) =
36
let n = next(l, dir) in
37
if is_linear(n)
38
then
39
if is_border(n) then return []
40
elsif hassignal(l, dir) \/ hassignal(n, opposite(dir))
41
then return [(l,n)]
42
else -- no signals between l and n
43
findcutfromlinear2(n, dir)
44
end
45
else -- is_point(n)
46
then return [(l,n)]
47

B.4
ﬁndcutfromstem
1
Functions should also have a net N as parameter, but left out here.
2
3
next(l, dir) is the neighbour of linear section l, in direction dir
4
next(p, branch) is the neighbour of point p, on the branch side
5
hassignal(l, dir) is true iff linear section l has a signal in direction dir
6
isconnected(p, plus, minus) is true iff plus and minus branches are connected in the
Graph of N
,!
7
8
-- input: p is a point, dir is a search direction (up or down)

-- output: a set of 1-2 cluster cuts

cut-minus = findcutfromstem-aux(p, minus, dir)

in

B.5
recursivebreak
1
The break function uses another function findcut(N, b).
2
It also uses a divide function that takes a network N and a cut and cuts the network
into two parts.
,!
3
4
findcut(N, b) returns a set of cluster cuts. A cluster cut is a set of single cuts.
5
A single cut is a pair of two neighbouring sections, in between where a cut should
be made. findcut(N,b) = {}, if a search from a border b in N is not successful
(due to a loop met from a branching side).
,!
,!
6
findcut(N,b) = {[]}, if N was elementary, so no cuts need to be made.
7
findcut(N,b) = {clustercut}, if a cluster cut is found, made of one or two single
cuts.
,!
8
findcut(N,b) = {clustercut1, clustercut2}, when two cluster cuts are found, each
made of one single cut.
,!
9
==PAGE:133==
B.5. RECURSIVEBREAK
123
10
findcut(N, b) = findcutfromlinear(b, dir(b))
11
-- where dir(b) gives the only direction in which b is connected to the network,
and N is given ads a hidden parameter to the function findcutfromlinear (as
explined in the definiton of the "findcutfrom" functions family.
,!
,!
12
13
input to break: N is a network, b a border in N (from which a search should start)
and bs a subset of the borders of N, b must not be in bs. bs is the set of
borders from which no search has yet been made.
,!
,!
14
15
break returns a pair of two sets of networks: the elementary ones of N, and the
unbreakable ones of N.
,!
16
17
This sentence needs to be reformulated
-->
assume here that divide returns two
nets, of which our algorithm guarantees that the first one is elementary.
,!
18
19
break(N, b, bs) =
20
let cutset = findcut(N,b)
in
21
if cutset = {[]} /* N is elementary */
then ({N}, {})
22
else
23
if cutset = {}
/* card cutset = 0: N is not breakable from b */
24
then /* try to search from another border b' */
25
if bs = {} then ({}, {N}) /* no other borders to search from */
26
else
let b' \in bs in break(N,b', bs\{b'}) end /* search from another
border */
,!
27
end
28
elsif
card cutset = 1
/* one cluster cut with 1 or 2 single cuts */
29
then
30
if the cluster cut
contains one single cut
31
then
32
let {[(t1,t2)]} = cutset, (N1, N2) = divide(N, [(t1,t2)]) in
33
({N1}, {}) union
34
break(N2,b1 /* t2 perhaps */, bs intersection borders(N2))
35
end
36
else
-- the clustercut
contains two single cuts
37
let {[(t1,t2), (t1',t2')]} = cutset, (N1, N2) = divide(N, [(t1,t2),
(t1',t2')]) in
,!
38
-- then N1 is elementary, continue with N2 from t1 (or t1'):
39
({N1}, {}) union
40
break(N2, b1, (bs intersection borders(N2)) union {b1'})
41
end
42
end
43
else /* card cutset = 2, two cluster cuts with 1 single cut each */
44
let
45
{[(t1,t2)], [(t1',t2')]} = cutset,
46
(N1, N2) = divide(N, [(t1,t2)]),
47
(N11, N12) = divide(N1, [(t1',t2')])
48
in
49
({N11}, {}) union
50
break(N2, b1, bs intersection borders(N2)) union
51
break(N12, b1', bs intersection borders(N12))
52
end
53
54
end
55
end
56
end
